Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Amputation When One Thief is Absent
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on amputation when one thief is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the shift in Imam Abu Hanifa's ruling on amputation when one thief is absent and identify the legal reasoning behind the later opinion.

Core Idea
Absence of a thief makes him legally non-existent, so his potential claim of doubt does not protect the present thief.

This passage addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute about the application of the hadd penalty for theft when two thieves are witnessed but one disappears before the trial. Imam Abu Hanifa initially held that the present thief should not be amputated because the absent might claim a doubt (shubha). He later retracted, and his new view became the relied-upon position of the school. Understanding this shift clarifies how Hanafi jurists weigh actual versus hypothetical doubts in hudud.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the initial ruling
Two people steal, both are witnessed, but one becomes absent before the hadd is applied. Abu Hanifa's earlier opinion said the present thief is not amputated.
The earlier opinion considered the possibility of shubha from the absent.
2
Understand the earlier reasoning
If the absent were present, he might claim a doubt that could protect both thieves (e.g., claiming ownership). The earlier opinion gave weight to this hypothetical.
Shubha in hudud generally prevents the hadd.
3
Trace the shift to the later opinion
Abu Hanifa later changed his view: absence prevents the theft from being proven against the absent, so he is legally non-existent (ma'dum). A non-existent person cannot cause shubha for the present thief.
The later opinion also became the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
4
Understand the underlying principle
Only actual shubha, not mere hypothetical possibilities, affects the application of hudud. The absent's absence makes any potential claim unreal.
This aligns with the general Hanafi principle that doubts must be established, not assumed.
5
Note the practical consequence
The present thief is amputated despite the absent's absence. The earlier opinion is no longer acted upon.
The mufta bihi is the later opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ سَرَقَا ثُمَّ غَابَ أَحَدُهُمَا وَشَهِدَ الشَّاهِدَانِ عَلَى سَرَقَتِهِمَا قُطِعَ الْآخَرُ فِي قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ الْآخِرِ وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُمَا وَكَانَ يَقُولُ أَوَّلاً لَا يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ حَضَرَ رُبَّمَا يَدَّعِي الشُّبْهَةَ وَجْهُ قَوْلِهِ الْآخِرِ أَنَّ الْغَيْبَةَ تَمْنَعُ ثُبُوتَ السَّرِقَةِ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ فَيَبْقَى مَعْدُومًا وَالْمَعْدُومُ لَا يُورِثُ الشُّبْهَةَ وَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ تَوَهٌّمُ حُدُوثِ الشُّبْهَةِ عَلَى مَا مَرَّ
If two (persons) steal, then one of them disappears, and two witnesses testify to their theft, the other (present) is amputated in the later opinion of Abu Hanifa, and it is the opinion of both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). He used to say earlier: He is not amputated, because if he (the absent) were present, he might claim a doubt. The reasoning of his later opinion: absence prevents the theft from being proven against the absent one, so he remains non-existent, and the non-existent does not cause shubha; and the mere imagining of a possible doubt is not considered, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf (dispute within the school) about a scenario where two people steal, both are witnessed, but one is absent. Imam Abu Hanifa initially held that the present thief should not be amputated because the absent thief might have claimed a shubha that would have protected both. He later retracted to the opinion that the present thief is amputated, and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agreed. The reasoning: the absent person's theft is not proven due to absence, so he is legally non-existent. A non-existent person cannot create shubha for the present thief. Similarly, hypothetical claims are not considered.

Key Terms
غائب (gha'ib): An absent person whose whereabouts are unknown; legally considered non-existent for the purpose of establishing theft.
معدوم (ma'dum): Non-existent; someone who cannot be reached or proven against.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two thieves steal, both are witnessed, but one is absent. According to Imam Abu Hanifa's later opinion, what is the ruling?
A) The present thief is not amputated because the absent may have claimed a doubt.
B) The present thief is amputated because the absent is legally non-existent and cannot create shubha.
C) The present thief is amputated only if the absent does not appear within a certain time.
D) Both are amputated after the absent is found.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi opinion establishes that only actual, not hypothetical, doubts can prevent the application of hudud, reflecting a principle of legal certainty.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Confession of an Interdicted Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on amputation of a mahjur slave who confesses to stealing ten dirhams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on amputation of a mahjur slave who confesses to theft and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to theft has different effects on hadd and property; the master's denial splits the Hanafi school.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. When a mahjur (interdicted) slave confesses to stealing specific goods, the master denies the confession. The key question: is the confession effective for the hadd penalty (amputation) and for the property claim? Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (with Zufar) each give different answers, highlighting the tension between the rights of Allah (hadd) and the rights of individuals (property).

Guided Path
1
State the three positions
List each imam's ruling on amputation and the disposal of the stolen goods. Abu Hanifa: cut and return to victim. Abu Yusuf: cut and goods to master. Muhammad/Zufar: no cut and goods to master.
2
Identify the reasoning for each
For hadd: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider the confession valid for hadd because it is a right of Allah; Muhammad and Zufar consider it invalid because the slave is interdicted and the master's denial overrides. For property: Abu Hanifa returns to the victim because the slave specified the goods; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad give to the master because the slave cannot dispose of property.
The master's denial defeats the slave's ability to transfer property, but opinions differ on whether it defeats the hadd.
3
Compare the positions
Note that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf agree on amputation but disagree on property. Muhammad and Zufar differ from both by rejecting amputation. The rift is about the effect of interdiction on confession.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical outcome differs: under Abu Hanifa, the victim gets the goods and the slave loses his hand; under Abu Yusuf, the master gets the goods and the slave loses his hand; under Muhammad, the master gets the goods and the slave keeps his hand.
The choice of position affects the master's financial interest and the severity of punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَقَرَّ الْعَبْدُ الْمَحْجُورُ عَلَيْهِ بِسَرِقَةِ عَشَرَةِ دَرَاهِمَ بِعَيْنِهَا فَإِنَّهُ يُقْطَعُ وَتُرَدُّ السَّرِقَةُ إِلَى الْمَسْرُوقِ مِنْهُ وَهَذَا عِندَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ يُقْطَعُ وَالْعَشَرَةُ لِلْمَوْلَى وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ لَا يُقْطَعُ وَالْعَشَرَةُ لِلْمَوْلَى وَهُوَ قَوْلُ زُفَرَ وَمَعْنَى هَذَا إِذَا كَذَّبَهُ الْمَوْلَى
And if a slave who is interdicted (mahjur 'alayhi) confesses to stealing ten specific dirhams, then he is amputated, and the stolen goods are returned to the victim – this is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf said: He is amputated, and the ten (dirhams) go to the master. Muhammad said: He is not amputated, and the ten go to the master – and this is also the saying of Zufar. And the meaning of this (is when) the master denies him (the slave's confession).
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a slave who is interdicted (forbidden from business) and confesses to stealing ten specific dirhams. The master denies the confession. Abu Hanifa: the slave's hand is cut, and the stolen goods are returned to the original owner. Abu Yusuf: the hand is cut, but the stolen goods go to the master (the slave's owner), not the victim, because the slave's confession is valid regarding himself (hadd) but not regarding property (the dirhams become the master's as they are under his control). Imam Muhammad and Zufar: the hand is not cut at all, because the slave's confession is completely ineffective due to the master's denial; the ten dirhams go to the master. The common thread: the master's denial negates the slave's ability to confess to property rights, but opinions differ on whether the hadd remains.

Key Terms
عبد محجور عليه (abd mahjur 'alayhi): A slave who is legally interdicted from financial transactions; his confession regarding property is not binding on the master.
مولى (mawla): Master or owner of the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The slave's hand is cut, and the dirhams are returned to the victim.
B) The slave's hand is cut, and the dirhams go to the master.
C) The slave's hand is not cut, and the dirhams go to the master.
D) The slave is not punished but must compensate the victim.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, differences on the effect of interdiction on confession produce three distinct rulings on amputation and property disposal.

Two Men Confess to Theft, One Retracts: The Hanafi Ruling on Shubha
Ruling on theft confession when one claimant retracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on joint confession with retraction and explain how shubha prevents the hadd punishment.

Core Idea
If one confessor retracts, both are spared due to shubha—uncertainty suspends the hadd.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses an important nuance in hudud law: what happens when two people confess to a theft and one later claims the property is his? The Hanafi school uses the principle of shubha (doubt) to prevent the harsh punishment of amputation. Understanding this ruling reveals how deeply the concept of shubha shapes Hanafi criminal jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial situation
Two men confess to a theft. The law presumes the theft is established by their joint confession, treating them as partners in the crime.
2
Recognize the retraction event
One of them says 'It is my property.' This amounts to a retraction of the confession, as he now claims ownership rather than admitting theft.
3
Apply the shubha principle
The retraction creates uncertainty (shubha) about the truth of the original confession. The Hanafi maxim 'al-hudud tudra'u bi al-shubahat' (hadd punishments are averted by doubts) applies.
4
Derive the ruling for both
Since the theft was established as a joint act, the doubt affects both confessors equally. Therefore, neither man's hand is amputated, even though the other did not retract.
5
Contrast with other schools (optional)
The Shafi'i school holds that only the retracting confessor is spared; the non-retracting confessor may still be amputated. This step highlights the practical difference.
Advanced understanding

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَقَرَّ رَجُلَانِ بِسَرْقَةٍ ثُمَّ قَالَ أَحَدُهُمَا هُوَ مَالِي لَمْ يُقْطَعْ لِلشُّبْهَةِ فِي حَقِّ الْآخَرِ لِأَنَّ السَّرْقَةَ تَثْبُتُ بِإِقْرَارِهِمَا عَلَى الشَّرِكَةِ
And if two men confess to a theft, then one of them says, 'It is my property,' the hand is not cut due to the doubt (shubha) concerning the other's right, because the theft is established by their joint confession.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the chapter on what requires amputation. The issue: two individuals confess to stealing a specific property, then one claims it was his own property. The ruling: the hand of neither is amputated. The reasoning: the confession of the second person initially established the theft as a joint act, but the retraction of the first creates a doubt (shubha) regarding the involvement of the other. Since hadd punishments are suspended by doubts, the amputation is not carried out. This is a clear application of the Hanafi principle that hadd penalties are averted by shubha.

Key Terms
الشُّبْهَة (al-shubhah): Uncertainty that prevents the application of hadd punishment, here created by one confessor's retraction affecting the other's liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two men confess to stealing a garment. One then says the garment is his own. What happens to the other man's hand in the Hanafi school?
A) His hand is cut because he did not retract his confession.
B) His hand is not cut because the retraction creates shubha for both.
C) His hand is cut only if the garment is worth more than 10 dirhams.
D) His hand is not cut only if he also retracts his confession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi jurisprudence, when two confess to theft and one retracts claiming ownership, the hadd is averted for both due to the doubt (shubha) created by the retraction, because the original confession was joint.

Two Thieves, One Absent: Cutting the Present Thief
Intra-Hanafi dispute: cutting the present thief when co-thief is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the final Hanafi ruling on cutting a present thief when his co-thief is absent and explain why hypothetical shubha does not avert the hadd.

Core Idea
Absence of a co-thief does not create a hypothetical shubha; the present thief is cut because the absent thief is legally non-existent.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about applying the hadd of theft when one of two thieves is absent and cannot be tried. Abu Hanifa initially hesitated but later, with the Sahibayn, ruled that the present thief's hand must be cut. The key legal insight is that only actual shubha blocks the hadd, not the mere possibility that the absent thief might have claimed one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Two persons steal together. One goes missing before trial. Witnesses testify against both. The question: Should the hand of the present thief be cut?
The absent thief cannot be tried due to absence.
2
State Abu Hanifa’s Earlier Opinion
Abu Hanifa initially said the present thief is not cut, reasoning that if the absent thief were present, he might claim a shubha that would protect both.
This view considered the potential future shubha.
3
State the Final Opinion and Its Proof
Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier view. The final opinion, also held by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, is that the present thief is cut. The proof: absence prevents establishing theft against the absent person, making him legally non-existent. A non-existent person cannot create shubha, and mere imagination of a future shubha is not considered.
The ruling relies on actual shubha, not hypothetical.
4
Identify the Tarjih and Practical Consequence
The final view is mufta bihi (relied upon). Practical consequence: In such cases, the present thief is cut; the earlier view would have spared him.
The later consensus of the three imams strengthens the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ سَرَقَا ثُمَّ غَابَ أَحَدُهُمَا وَشَهِدَ الشَّاهِدَانِ عَلَى سَرَقَتِهِمَا قُطِعَ الْآخَرُ فِي قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ الْآخِرِ وَهُوَ قَوْلُهُمَا وَكَانَ يَقُولُ أَوَّلًا لَا يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ حَضَرَ رُبَمَا يَدَّعِي الشُّبْهَةَ وَجْهُ قَوْلِهِ الْآخِرِ أَنَّ الْغَيْبَةَ تَمْنَعُ ثُبُوتَ السَّرْقَةِ عَلَى الْغَائِبِ فَيَبْقَى مَعْدُومًا وَالْمَعْدُومُ لَا يُورِثُ الشُّبْهَةَ وَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ تَوَهُمُ حُدُوثِ الشُّبْهَةِ
If two persons steal, then one of them disappears, and two witnesses testify to their theft, the other (present one) is cut according to the final opinion of Abu Hanifa, and it is also the opinion of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). He initially used to say he is not cut, because if he were present, perhaps he would claim a doubt (shubha). The basis of his final opinion is that absence prevents the establishment of theft against the absent person, so he remains non-existent, and the non-existent does not engender doubt, nor is the mere imagination of the occurrence of doubt considered.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a situation where two persons steal together, one goes missing, and witnesses testify against both. Abu Hanifa first held that the present thief should not be amputated because the absent thief, if present, might claim a shubha that would affect both. However, he later retracted this and, along with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, ruled that the present thief is cut. The reason: since the absent thief cannot be tried, his potential claims are non-existent and cannot create a real shubha for the present thief. The mere possibility of a future claim does not avert the hadd. This reinforces the Hanafi principle that shubha must be actual, not hypothetical.

Key Terms
الْغَيْبَة (al-ghaybah): Physical absence of a person to the extent that legal proceedings against them cannot be conducted.
الشُّبْهَة (al-shubhah): A doubt or ambiguity that can avert a hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two thieves steal together. One goes missing. Witnesses testify against both. According to the final Hanafi position, what is the ruling?
A) The present thief's hand is not cut because the absent thief might have claimed a doubt.
B) The present thief's hand is cut because absence renders the absent thief legally non-existent.
C) Both thieves' hands are cut if the stolen property is found.
D) The present thief's hand is cut only if the absent thief is proven dead.

Key Takeaway
The present thief is cut because absence renders the absent thief legally non-existent, eliminating any potential shubha.

Interdicted Slave's Confession of Theft: Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on theft confession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad/Zufar) regarding amputation and property return for an interdicted slave's theft confession, and explain the condition of the master's denial that triggers the dispute.

Core Idea
An interdicted slave's theft confession splits Hanafi imams: amputation yes/no, property to victim or master.

The passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute where a single scenario—an interdicted slave confesses to theft of specific dirhams—yields three distinct rulings among the founding imams. The disagreement centers on the slave's legal capacity and the master's property rights. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to see how different usul principles produce varied fiqh outcomes even within one madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
The case is an interdicted slave (mahjūr 'alayhi) who confesses to stealing ten specific dirhams. The master denies the confession. Identify the actors: slave, victim, master.
2
State the three positions
List the rulings of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad/Zufar on two issues: amputation and to whom the stolen property goes.
Each position is a combination of these two variables.
3
Identify the condition
The passage states that this ikhtilaf applies 'when the master denies' the confession. Determine what would happen if the master confirmed the confession (consensus mentioned elsewhere).
The master's denial is the trigger for disagreement.
4
Analyze the legal rationale
Consider the underlying principles: the slave's capacity to confess, the master's ownership of the slave's acquisitions, and the nature of hadd punishment. Why might Muhammad deny amputation while Abu Hanifa affirms it?
Reasoning is not fully given in the passage but can be inferred.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah)
Focus on the real-world outcomes: property returned to victim vs. property stays with master; amputation applied or not. This shows how the dispute affects both the hadd and property rights.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text

وَإِذَا أَقَرَّ الْعَبْدُ الْمَحْجُورُ عَلَيْهِ بِسَرِقَةِ عَشَرَةِ دَرَاهِمَ بِعَيْنِهَا فَإِنَّهُ يُقْطَعُ وَتُرَدُّ السَّرِقَةُ إِلَى الْمَسْرُوقِ مِنْهُ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ يُقْطَعُ وَالْعَشَرَةُ لِلْمَوْلَى وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ لَا يُقْطَعُ وَالْعَشَرَةُ لِلْمَوْلَى وَهُوَ قَوْلُ زُفَرَ وَمَعْنَى هَذَا إِذَا كَذَّبَهُ الْمَوْلَى

And this is when the master denies (the confession).
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the effect of a theft confession by an interdicted slave (mahjūr 'alayhi). The disputed issues: amputation of the slave's hand and the ownership of the stolen property. Abu Hanifa: the slave is cut and the property is returned to the original owner. Abu Yusuf: the slave is cut but the property goes to the slave's master. Muhammad and Zufar: no amputation, and the property goes to the master. The ikhtilaf is conditioned on the master denying the confession (i.e., saying the slave is lying). If the master confirms the confession, there is consensus that the slave is cut (mentioned later). The reasoning revolves around the slave's capacity to confess and the master's ownership of the slave's actions.

Key Terms
الْمَحْجُورُ عَلَيْهِ (al-mahjūr 'alayhi): A slave who is interdicted from conducting business (muhjūr) due to being prevented by his master from commercial transactions.
إِقْرَار (iqrār): Confession; a legal admission that can establish liability or hadd.
قَطْع (qaṭ‘): Amputation of the hand as hadd punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Slave's Confession for Consumed Stolen Property: Intra-Hanafi Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cutting for consumed stolen property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar) on amputation when a slave confesses to stealing consumed property, and explain the principal-subsidiary reasoning used.

Core Idea
A slave's confession to hudud is valid despite the master's ownership; consumed property does not prevent amputation but affects property disposition.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about the intersection of hudud and slave law. Zufar challenges the validity of a slave's confession to amputation, while the majority agree that amputation applies but differ on property return. The key analytical tool is the principal-subsidiary (asl-far') relation between property and amputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial ruling and Zufar's objection
Note that the text opens with 'his hand is cut' for consumed property even from a slave. Zufar objects in all cases because he deems a slave's confession to hudud invalid—his body is the master's property.
Zufar's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to Zufar
The Hanafi response argues the slave is a human being capable of confession, and the harm (amputation) removes suspicion of collusion. This establishes the validity of the confession for amputation.
This response applies to both permitted (ma'dhūn) and interdicted (mahjūr) slaves initially.
3
Distinguish the three positions of Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, and Abu Hanifa
Muhammad holds that for the interdicted slave, property confession is void, and since property is principal, amputation falls. Abu Yusuf separates: amputation valid, property claim against master invalid. Abu Hanifa integrates: amputation valid, property follows as subsidiary.
These positions affect whether the victim or the master keeps the property value.
4
Apply the principal-subsidiary framework to understand Muhammad's view
Muhammad argues that property (mal) is the principal (asl) and amputation (qat') is the subsidiary (far'). If the principal claim is void, the subsidiary must also be void. For the permitted slave, the property confession is valid, so amputation stands.
This reasoning differs from Abu Hanifa's, who sees amputation as primary in the remaining state.
5
Note the effect of the master's confirmation
The text concludes that if the master confirms the slave's confession, amputation is applied in all cases because the preventer (master's objection) is removed.
This shows the master's acknowledgement can remove the legal impediment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوْ أَقَرَّ بِسَرْقَةِ مَالٍ مُسْتَهْلَكٍ قُطِعَتْ يَدُهُ وَلَوْ كَانَ الْعَبْدُ مَأْذُونًا لَهُ يُقْطَعُ فِي الْوَجْهَيْنِ وَقَالَ زُفَرُ لَا يُقْطَعُ فِي الْوُجُوهِ كُلِّهَا لِأَنَّ الْأَصْلَ عِنْدَهُ أَنَّ إِقْرَارَ الْعَبْدِ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ بِالْحُدُودِ وَالْقِصَاصِ لَا يَصِحُّ لِأَنَّهُ يُرَدُّ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ وَطَرْفِهِ وَكُلُّ ذَلِكَ مَالُ الْمَوْلَى وَالْإِقْرَارُ عَلَى الْغَيْرِ بِالْمَالِ أَيْضًا وَنَحْنُ نَقُولُ يَصِحُّ إِقْرَارُهُ مِنْ حَيْثُ أَنَّهُ آدَمِيٌّ ثُمَّ يَتَعَدَّى إِلَى الْمَالِيَّةِ فَيَصِحُّ مِنْ حَيْثُ إِنَّهُ مَالٌ وَلِأَنَّهُ لَا تُهْمَةَ فِي هَذَا الْإِقْرَارِ لِمَا يَشْتَمِلُ عَلَيْهِ مِنَ الْأَضْرَارِ وَمِثْلُهُ مَقْبُولٌ عَلَى الْغَيْرِ وَعِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ فِي الْمَحْجُورِ عَلَيْهِ أَنَّ إِقْرَارَهُ بِالْمَالِ بَاطِلٌ وَلِهَذَا لَا يَصِحُّ مِنْهُ الْإِقْرَارُ بِالْغَصْبِ فَيَبْقَى مَالُ الْمَوْلَى وَلَا قَطْعَ عَلَى الْعَبْدِ فِي سَرِقَةِ مَالِ الْمَوْلَى يُؤَيِّدُهُ أَنَّ الْمَالَ أَصْلٌ فِيهَا وَالْقَطْعُ تَابِعٌ حَتَّى تُسْمَعَ الْخُصُومَةُ فِيهِ بِدُونِ الْقَطْعِ وَيَثْبُتُ الْمَالُ دُونَهُ وَفِي عَكْسِهِ لَا تُسْمَعُ وَلَا يَثْبُتُ وَإِذَا بَطَلَ فِيمَا هُوَ الْأَصْلُ بَطَلَ فِي التَّبِعِ بِخِلَافِ الْمَأْذُونِ لِأَنَّ إِقْرَارَهُ بِالْمَالِ الَّذِي فِي يَدِهِ صَحِيحٌ فَيَصِحُّ فِي حَقِّ الْقَطْعِ تَبَعًا وَعِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ أَنَّهُ أَقَرَّ بِشَيْئَيْنِ بِالْقَطْعِ وَهُوَ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ فَيَصِحُّ عَلَى مَا ذَكَرْنَاهُ وَبِالْمَالِ وَهُوَ عَلَى الْمَوْلَى فَلَا يَصِحُّ فِي حَقِّهِ فِيهِ وَالْقَطْعُ يُسْتَحَقُّ بِدُونِهِ كَمَا إِذَا قَالَ الْحُرُّ الثَّوْبُ الَّذِي فِي يَدِ زَيْدٍ سَرَقْتُهُ مِنْ عَمْرٍو وَزَيْدٌ يَقُولُ هُوَ ثَوْبِي يُقْطَعُ يَدُ الْمُقِرِّ وَإِنْ كَانَ لَا يُصَدَّقُ فِي تَعْيِينِ الثَّوْبِ حَتَّى لَا يُؤْخَذَ مِنْ زَيْدٍ وَعِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ أَنَّ الْإِقْرَارَ بِالْقَطْعِ قَدْ صَحَّ مِنْهُ لِمَا بَيَّنَّا فَيَصِحُّ بِالْمَالِ بِنَاءً عَلَيْهِ لِأَنَّ الْإِقْرَارَ يُلَاقِي حَالَةَ الْبَقَاءِ وَالْمَالُ فِي حَالَةِ الْبَقَاءِ تَابِعٌ لِلْقَطْعِ حَتَّى تَسْقُطَ عِصْمَةُ الْمَالِ بِاعْتِبَارِهِ وَيُسْتَوْفَى الْقَطْعُ بَعْدَ اسْتِهْلَاكِهِ بِخِلَافِ مَسْأَلَةِ الْحُرِّ لِأَنَّ الْقَطْعَ إِنَّمَا يَجِبُ بِالسَّرْقَةِ مِنَ الْمُودَعِ أَمَّا لَا يَجِبُ بِسَرِقَةِ الْعَبْدِ مَالَ الْمَوْلَى فَافْتَرَقَا وَلَوْ صَدَّقَهُ الْمَوْلَى يُقْطَعُ فِي الْفُصُولِ كُلِّهَا لِزَوَالِ الْمَانِعِ
And if he confesses to the theft of consumed property, his hand is cut. And if the slave is permitted (ma'dhūn), he is cut in both cases. Zufar said: He is not cut in all cases, because the principle with him is that a slave's confession against himself regarding hudud and qisas is not valid, as it reflects on his own self and his limbs, and all of that is the property of the master. Confession against others is not accepted except for the permitted slave, who is held liable for guarantee and property because his confession is valid since he is authorized over it. The interdicted slave's confession regarding property is also invalid. We say: his confession is valid because he is a human being, then it extends to property, so it is valid from the property aspect, and because there is no suspicion of collusion in this confession due to the harm it entails. The like is accepted against others. According to Muhammad regarding the interdicted slave: his confession regarding property is void, and therefore his confession of usurpation (ghasb) is not valid, so the master's property remains, and there is no amputation on the slave in stealing the master's property. This is supported by the fact that the property is the principal in this case and amputation is subsidiary, such that litigation is heard concerning it without amputation, and the property is established without it. In the opposite case (amputation without property), it is not heard and is not established. And if it is void in that which is principal, it is void in the subsidiary. Contrary to the permitted slave, because his confession regarding property in his hand is valid, so it is valid regarding amputation as subsidiary. According to Abu Yusuf, he confessed to two things: amputation against himself, so it is valid according to what we mentioned; and property against the master, so it is not valid regarding his right in it. And amputation is deserved without it, just as when a free person says, 'The garment in Zaid's hand I stole from 'Amr,' and Zaid says, 'It is my garment,' the hand of the confessor is cut, even though he is not believed regarding the identification of the garment such that it would be taken from Zaid. According to Abu Hanifa, the confession to amputation is valid from him for the reasons we explained, so the confession regarding property is valid as based upon it, because the confession meets the remaining state (after consumption) and the property in the remaining state is subsidiary to amputation, such that the inviolability of property falls by its consideration, and amputation is executed after its consumption. Contrary to the case of the free person, because amputation is only obligatory for theft from a depository, whereas it is not obligatory for the slave's theft of the master's property, so they differ. And if the master confirms him (the slave), he is cut in all cases due to the removal of the preventer (al-māni').
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an extended intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a slave's confession to theft of property that has been consumed (mustahlak). The discussion distinguishes between an interdicted slave (mahjūr) and a permitted slave (ma'dhūn). The ruling: if a slave confesses to consuming stolen property, his hand is cut. This applies whether the slave is mahjūr or ma'dhūn, according to the early Hanafi view responding to Zufar. Zufar argues that no amputation applies in any case because a slave's confession to hudud is invalid since his body is his master's property. The Hanafi response: the slave's confession is valid because he is a human being, and the harm it causes (amputation) negates any suspicion of collusion. The passage then details the positions of Muhammad, Abu Yusuf, and Abu Hanifa. Muhammad holds that for the interdicted slave, property confession is void, so amputation also falls because property is the principal and amputation is subsidiary. Abu Yusuf separates the two: amputation is valid, but the property claim against the master is not, analogous to a free person's misidentified theft confession. Abu Hanifa integrates them: amputation is valid, and property follows amputation in the remaining state, so the victim gets the property. The final sentence notes that if the master confirms the slave's confession, amputation is applied in all cases because the preventer (the master's denial) is removed.

Key Terms
الْمَأْذُونُ (al-ma'dhūn): A slave who has been granted permission (idhn) by his master to engage in trade, making his commercial confessions valid.
الْمَحْجُورُ عَلَيْهِ (al-mahjūr 'alayhi): An interdicted slave, prevented from trade; his confessions regarding property are generally invalid according to Muhammad.
مُسْتَهْلَكٌ (mustahlak): Property that has been consumed or destroyed, such that it cannot be returned in kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental reason Zufar gives for not amputating a slave who confesses to theft?
A) The slave's confession is not credible without witnesses.
B) The stolen property was consumed, so no penalty applies.
C) The slave's body is the property of the master, so confessing to amputating it is confessing against the master's property.
D) The slave is not legally responsible for hudud crimes.

Key Takeaway
A slave's confession to amputation is valid despite the master's ownership; the consumption of property does not prevent amputation, but the property disposition depends on the imam's view on the principal-subsidiary link.

Slave's Confession of Theft: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Amputation
Explaining the ruling on a slave's confession of theft and its effect on amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on a slave's confession of theft and identify which is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A slave's confession of theft affects both amputation and property according to Abu Hanifa; the Sahibayn limit its effect.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal effect of a slave's confession of theft. While all three imams of the school—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—agree on the basic principles of iqrar, they differ on whether such a confession can bind the master in matters of property. This lesson dissects their arguments and highlights the practical implications of their disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal context
The act is a slave's confession of theft. The context involves whether the slave is restricted (mahjur) or permitted to trade, as this affects the validity of the confession regarding property.
The passage focuses on the restricted slave; the permitted slave is treated as an exception.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the confession is valid for both the hadd (amputation) and the property liability. He considers the amputation as primary and the property claim as subsidiary.
Key dalil: after amputation, the thief is not liable for destroyed property, showing primacy of hadd.
3
State the Sahibayn's positions
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that a restricted slave's confession regarding property is void, so neither amputation nor liability follows. Imam Abu Yusuf distinguishes: the confession regarding amputation (affecting the slave) is valid, but regarding property (affecting the master) is invalid.
Muhammad's reasoning: a slave's hand is not cut for stealing master's property. Abu Yusuf's reasoning: the confession has two separate effects.
4
Analyse the proofs and counterproofs
Examine the dalil for each view, including analogies to the free person case and the distinction regarding the master's property. Note how Abu Hanifa responds by emphasizing the primary/subsidiary relationship.
The free person analogy (Abu Yusuf) is countered by Abu Hanifa's distinction that a slave's situation is different.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and the mufta bihi
The practical consequences differ: under Abu Hanifa, amputation and liability; under Muhammad, neither; under Abu Yusuf, amputation only. The mufta bihi (operative position) is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is the foundation of the school.
If the master disowns the property, the hand is cut in all views because the obstacle is removed.

باب ما يقطع فيه و ما لا يقطع . مال پر ہوا اور اپ اقرار جس کا اثر کسی غیر پر واقع ہوہ مقبول نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ (یہاں تک کہ اگر غلام یہ اقرار کرے کہ میں فلاں شخص کا غلام ہوں یعنی اپنے بولی کے سوا کسی دوسرے کا غلام جلا دے تو اقرار باطل ہوتا ہے۔ البتہ جس غلام کو اس کے مولی نے تجارت کرنے کی اجازت دی ہے جب چوری کرنے کا اقرار کرے اور وہ مال ہلاک ہو چکا ہو اس کے تاوان میں اسے پکڑا جائے گا اور اگر مال موجود ہو تو اس کے واپس لوٹانے کے لئے اسے پکڑا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ مال سے متعلق اس کا اقرار صحیح ہے۔ اس لئے کہ خود اس کے مولی نے اسے اس مال کے لین دین پر مسلط کر دیا ہے اور اجازت دے رکھی ہے۔ لیکن مجبور غلام ( جسے کاروبار سے منع کیا گیا ہو) کے مال سے بھی اقرار صحیح نہیں ہوتا ہے اور ہم یہ کہتے ہیں کہ غلام ہے کہ وہ بھی ایک آدمی ہے۔ پھر یہ اقرار مال کی طرف منتقل ہو جاتا ہے تو یہ بھی اس اعتبار سے صحیح ہے کہ وہ مال ہے اور اس لئے بھی کہ اس اقرار میں کوئی تہمت بھی لازم نہیں آتی ہے کیونکہ اقرار کی وجہ سے اس کا نقصان ہی ہوتا ہے ( نفع نہیں ہوتا ہے ) اور ایسا دوسرے کے حق میں بھی مقبول ہو جاتا ہے اور امام محمد کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ جو غلام مجبور ہے اس کا مال سے متعلق اقرار باطل ہوتا ہے۔ اسی وجہ سے اس کی طرف سے غصب کا اقرار صحیح نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ ( مثلا مولی کے پاس یہ کہے کہ میں نے یہ مال فلاں شخص کا غصب کیا ہے۔ تو صحیح نہ ہوگا) لہذا مولی کا مال باقی رہ جائے گا اور یہ مال اس کے مولی کا مال رہ جائے گا اور چونکہ اس مال کے چرانے کا اقرار کرنا تھا اس لئے اس کا ہاتھ کاٹا بھی نہیں جائے گا۔ کیونکہ مولی کا مال چرانے سے غلام کا ہاتھ نہیں کاٹا جاتا ہے اور اس کی تائید اس طرح سے ہوتی ہے کہ چوری کرنے میں اصل مال ہے اور اس کی وجہ سے چور کا ہاتھ کاٹ جانا اس کے تابع ہے۔ اسی بناء پر ایسی صورت میں بھی مالی شکایت اور خصوصیت ہو سکتی ہے جہاں ہاتھ کاٹنے کی کوئی صورت نہ ہو اور قطع کے بغیر بھی مال ثابت ہوتا ہے اور اگر اس کے برعکس ہو یعنی مال کا مطالبہ نہ ہو بلکہ قطع کا مطالبہ ہو تو نالش کی سماعت نہ ہوگی اور ثبوت نہ ہوگا۔ نہیں مسئلہ کی جس صورت میں اصل ہی باطل ہو یعنی مال کا بھی ثبوت نہ ہو تو چیز اس مال کے تابع ہوگی یعنی قطع ید وہ بھی باطل ہوگی۔ بخلاف ایسے غلام کے جس کو تجارت کی اجازت ملی ہوئی ہوں۔ کیونکہ ایسے غلام کے قبضہ میں جو مال ہوگا اسکے متعلق اس غلام کا اقرار صحیح ہوگا۔ اسلئے اسکا تابع یعنی قطع ید کے حق میں بھی یہ اقرار صحیح ہو گا اور امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام نے دو باتوں کا اقرار کیا ہے ایک تو ہاتھ کاٹنے کی سزا کا حالانکہ اس اقرار کا اثر خود اس کی ذات پر پڑتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ ہم نے پہلے ذکر کر دیا ہے اور دوسرے مال کا اقرار کیا ہے اور اس مال کے اقرار کا اثر اسکے مولی پر پڑتا ہے اسلئے مولی کے حق میں اس کا اقرار درست نہ ہوگا اور ہاتھ کاٹنے کے مطالبہ کا حق مال کے بغیر بھی ہوتا ہے۔ مثلاً کسی آزاد شخص نے کہا۔ کہ یہ کپڑا جو زید کے ہاتھ میں ہے میں نے اسے خالد کے گھر سے چرایا ہے اور زید کہتا ہے کہ یہ کپڑا میرا ہے۔ تو اقراری چور کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ اگر چہ اس کپڑے سے متعلق اس کی بات کی تصدیق نہیں ہوگی اور وہ زید سے نہیں چھینا جائے گا اور امام ابوحنیفہ کی دلیل (اس دعوی کی چوری کے مسئلہ میں ہاتھ کا کاٹا جانا ) اصل ہے اور مال اسکے تابع ہے اسی لئے اگر چور نے مال ضائع کر دیا ہو تو اسکا ہاتھ کاٹ دیئے جانے کے بعد اب وہ اس مال کا ضامن نہیں ہوگا) پس غلام کی طرف سے قطع کا اقرار صحیح ہو چکا ہے کیونکہ وہ بھی آدمی ہے۔ اسی بناء پر مال کا اقرار بھی صحیح ہے۔ کیونکہ اقرار کا تعلق ایسی حالت سے متصل ہوتا ہے جو باقی ہے اور باقی ہونے کی حالت میں مال تابع اور قطع اصل ہوتا ہے۔ یہاں تک کہ اسی قطع کی بنیاد پر مال کی عصمت ( اور ذاتی عزت ) ختم ہو جاتی ہے اور اگر چور نے اس مال کو ضائع کر دیا ہو تو بھی قطع ید کی سزا پوری کر لی جاتی ہے اور بخلاف آزاد کے مسئلہ کے یعنی وہ غلام کے مسئلہ کی نظیر نہیں ہے کیونکہ امانت دار کے پاس سے بھی مال چرانے پر بھی قطع واجب ہوتا ہے اور اگر غلام اپنے مولی کا مال چرا لے تو اسکا ہاتھ کاٹنا واجب نہیں ہوتا ہے اسطرح دونوں میں فرق ظاہر ہو گیا۔ البتہ اگر مولی نے ان دونوں صورتوں میں تصدیق کر دی یعنی یہ کہہ دیا کہ یہ مال میرا نہیں ہے بلکہ یہ غلام کہیں سے چرا کر لایا ہے۔ تو مذکورہ تمام صورتوں میں غلام کا ہاتھ کاٹا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ جو چیز حائل ہو رہی تھی اب وہ باقی نہیں رہی۔
Chapter on what necessitates amputation and what does not. Regarding a confession whose effect falls upon another, it is not accepted. (For example, if a slave confesses that he is the slave of so-and-so, i.e., he claims to be the slave of someone other than his master, his confession is invalid. However, if a slave who has been given permission by his master to trade confesses to theft and the property is already destroyed, he will be held liable for its value; if the property still exists, he will be held to return it. Because his confession regarding property is valid, since his master has authorized him to deal with property. But for a restricted slave (who is forbidden from business), his confession regarding property is not valid. And we say that the slave is also a human being. Then this confession transfers to the property, so it is valid in that respect because it is property, and also because there is no accusation implied in this confession, since it only harms him (does not benefit him). And such a confession is accepted even regarding the rights of others. The proof of Imam Muhammad is that the confession of a restricted slave regarding property is void. For this reason, his confession of usurpation is not valid (for example, if he says to his master, 'I have usurped this property from so-and-so,' it is not valid). Therefore, his master's property remains as is, and since his confession was about stealing this property, his hand will not be cut. Because a slave's hand is not cut for stealing his master's property. This is supported by the principle that in theft, the primary concern is the property, and the cutting of the hand is subsidiary. For this reason, there can be a property claim and specificity in a case where there is no possibility of cutting, and the property is proven without cutting. Conversely, if there is no claim for property but only a claim for cutting, the lawsuit will not be heard and no proof will be established. In a case where the primary is invalid (i.e., property is not proven), then the subsidiary (cutting) will also be invalid. As opposed to a slave who has been given permission to trade, because regarding the property in his possession, his confession is valid; therefore, the subsidiary, i.e., the right of cutting, his confession is also valid. The proof of Imam Abu Yusuf is that the slave confessed to two things: first, the punishment of cutting the hand, and this confession affects his own person; second, the confession regarding property, and this affects his master, so his confession is not valid regarding the master's right. The right to demand cutting exists even without property. For example, a free person said, 'This cloth which is in Zaid's hand, I stole it from Khalid's house,' and Zaid says, 'This cloth is mine.' Then the confessing thief's hand will be cut, even if his statement regarding the cloth is not confirmed and it will not be taken from Zaid. The proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that the primary (the cutting) is established, and the property is subsidiary. For this reason, if the thief has destroyed the property, after his hand is cut he is not liable for it. So the slave's confession of cutting is correct because he is also a human being. On this basis, his confession of property is also correct, because the confession is connected to a state that remains, and in that state, property is subsidiary and cutting is primary. To the extent that based on this cutting, the property's inviolability (and personal honor) is removed, and even if the thief destroyed the property, the punishment of cutting is fully carried out. This is unlike the case of a free person, which is not analogous to the slave's case, because cutting is obligatory even for stealing from a trustee, but if a slave steals from his master, cutting is not obligatory; thus the difference becomes clear. However, if the master confirms in both cases by saying, 'This property is not mine; rather, this slave brought it from somewhere,' then in all mentioned cases the slave's hand will be cut, because the obstacle no longer remains.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah is a commentary on the chapter on theft, specifically addressing the ruling when a slave confesses to theft. The issue is whether the slave's confession is effective for both the hadd punishment (amputation of the hand) and the liability for the stolen property. The text presents three positions from the Hanafi school. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that a restricted slave's confession regarding property is invalid, and consequently, his confession of theft does not lead to amputation because the hand of a slave is not cut for stealing his master's property. Imam Abu Yusuf distinguishes between the two aspects: the confession regarding the hand (which affects the slave himself) is valid, but the confession regarding property (which affects the master) is not valid in the master's right; however, the amputation can still be carried out based on the confession about the hand alone. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that both confessions are valid: the primary is the amputation (since the slave is a responsible person), and the property claim is subsidiary; therefore, the confession is valid for both. The passage also notes that if the master disowns the property (says it is not his), then the hand is cut in all cases. This dispute illustrates the Hanafi method of analyzing legal acts that have multiple effects on different parties. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as indicated by the overall structure of the Hidayah where his view is generally preferred.

Key Terms
اقرار (iqrar): Confession or acknowledgment of a right or liability.
مولی (mawla): Master of a slave; owner.
غلام (ghulam): A male slave; in this context, a slave owned by a master.
قطع ید (qat' al-yad): Amputation of the hand as a hadd punishment for theft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the effect of a restricted slave's confession of theft regarding the stolen property?
A) The confession is invalid, so the slave is not liable for the property and his hand is not cut.
B) The confession is valid for both amputation and property liability; the hand is cut and the slave must compensate the master.
C) The confession is valid only for the amputation, not for property liability.
D) The confession is valid only if the master confirms it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mufta bihi is that a slave's confession of theft is valid for both amputation and property liability, with amputation as the primary effect.

Liability of the Thief After Amputation: No Financial Obligation
Stating the ruling after amputation: stolen property returned if extant, no liability if destroyed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the thief's liability after amputation and explain the proofs from hadith and the principle of shubha.

Core Idea
After amputation, the thief is not liable for destroyed property; liability would create a shubha negating the hadd.

This passage addresses a key question in Islamic criminal law: once a thief's hand has been amputated (the hadd punishment), does he still owe compensation for the stolen property? The Hanafi school, relying on a specific hadith and logical reasoning about shubha, holds that he does not. This lesson unpacks the textual evidence, the internal Hanafi disagreement, and the rebuttal against the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Read the first few lines: after amputation, if the stolen property is still extant, it is returned; if destroyed (by any cause), the thief is not liable. This is the mashhur (famous) position from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa.
The text uses 'mustahlaka' covering both natural destruction (halak) and consumption (istihlak).
2
Locate the primary proof
Find the hadith cited: 'La gharama 'ala al-sariq ba'da ma quti'at yaminuhu' (No liability on the thief after his right hand is cut). Note its transmission status (ahad) and how it directly negates financial obligation.
The hadith is the strongest textual evidence for the Hanafi position.
3
Understand the rational proof (shubha)
The passage argues that imposing liability would allow the thief to become owner of the property by paying its value, retroactively creating a shubha (doubt) that would nullify the amputation. Since the hadd cannot be negated, liability is dropped.
This is a classic Hanafi use of shubha to preserve the hadd penalty.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab disagreements
Identify that Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that liability applies in case of intentional consumption (istihlak). Also note that Imam al-Shafi'i holds liability in both destruction and consumption, arguing the two rights (hadd and daman) have different causes.
The mashhur view (no liability) is the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يقطع فيه و مالا يقطع وَإِذَا قُطِعَ السَّارِقُ وَالْعَيْنُ قَائِمَةٌ فِي يَدِهِ رُدَّتْ إِلى صَاحِبِهَا لِبَقَائِهَا عَلَى مِلْكِهِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُسْتَهْلَكَةٌ لَمْ يَضْمَنْ وَهذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ يَشْمُلُ الْهَلَاكَ وَالْإِسْتِهْلَاكَ وَهُوَ رِوَايَةُ أَبِى يُوسُفٌ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَهُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ وَرَوَى الْحَسَنُ عَنْهُ أَنَّهُ يَضْمَنُ بِالْإِسْتِهْلَاكِ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ يَضْمَنُ فِيهِمَا لِأَنَّهُمَا حَقَّانِ قَدْ اخْتَلَفَ سَبَبُهُمَا فَلَايَمْتَعَانِ فَالْقَطْعُ حَقُّ الشَّرْعِ وَسَبَبُهُ تَرْكُ الْإِنْتِهَاءِ عَمَّا نُهِيَ عَنْهُ وَالصَّمَانُ حَقُّ الْعَبْدِ وَسَبَبُهُ أَخْدُ الْمَالِ فَصَارَ كَاسْتِهْلَاكِ صَيْدٍ مَمْلُوكِ فِي الْحَرَمِ اَوْشُرْبِ خَمْرٍ مَمْلُوْكَةِ لِلذَّمِّي وَلَنَا قَوْلُهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لَا غُرْمَ عَلَى السَّارِقِ بَعْدَ مَا قُطِعَتْ يَمِيْنُهُ وَلَا وُجُوبَ الصَّمَانِ فِينَا فِي الْقَطْعَ لِأَنَّهُ يَتَمَلَكُهُ بِاَدَاءِ الصَّمَانِ مُسْتَنِدًا إِلى وَقْتِ الْاخْذِ فَتَبَيَّنَ أَنَّهُ وَرَدَ عَلَى مِلْكِهِ فَيَسْقُطُ الْقَطْعُ لِلشَّبْهَةِ وَمَا يُؤَدِّي إِلَى انْتِفَاتِهِ فَهُوَ الْمُنْتَفَى وَلَانَ الْمَحَلَّ لَا يَبْقَى مَعْصُوْمًا حَقًّا لِلْعَبْدِ إِذْ لَوْ بَقِيَ لَكَانَ مُبَاحًا فِي نَفْسِهِ فَيَنْتَفِي الْقَطْعُ لِلشُّبْهَةِ فَيَصِيرُ مُحَرَّمًا حَقًّا لِلشَّرْعِ كَالْمَيْتَةِ وَلَا ضَمَانَ فِيهِ إِلَّا أَنَّ الْعِصْمَةَ لَا يَظْهَرُ سُقُوطُهَا فِي حَقِ الْإِسْتِهْلَاكِ لِأَنَّهُ فِعْلٌ آخَرُ غَيْرُ السَّرْقَةِ وَلَا ضُرُورَةَ فِي حَقِهِ وَكَذَا الشُّبْهَةُ تُعْتَبَرُ فِيمَا هُوَ السَّبَبُ دُونَ غَيْرِهِ وَوَجْهُ الْمَشْهُورُ أَنَّ الْإِسْتِهْلَاكَ إِثْمَامُ الْمَقْصُودِ فَيُعْتَبَرُ الشَّبْهَةُ فِيْهِ وَكَذَا يَظْهَرُ سُقُوطُ الْعِصْمَةِ فِي حَقِ الضَّمَانِ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ ضُرُورَاتِ سُقُوطِهَا فِى حَقِ الْهَلَاكِ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْمُمَاثَلَةِ
Chapter on what necessitates amputation and what does not. And when the thief's hand is cut and the stolen item is still in his possession, it is returned to its owner because it remains in the owner's ownership. If it is destroyed (mustahlaka or halāk), he is not liable. This absolute statement includes both natural destruction and consumption/destruction by the thief. This is the narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, and it is the famous (mashhur) position. Al-Hasan narrated from him that he is liable for consumption. Al-Shafi'i said: he is liable in both cases, because both are rights whose causes differ, so they do not prevent each other: amputation is a right of the Shari'ah, its cause is violating the prohibition, while liability is a right of the subject (the victim), its cause is taking property. It is like the case of consuming owned game in the Haram or drinking wine owned by a dhimmi. Our proof is his (peace be upon him) statement: 'There is no liability on the thief after his right hand is cut.' Also, the obligation of liability is incompatible with amputation because if the thief pays compensation, he becomes owner of the property from the time of taking, so it becomes clear that he took his own property, and amputation would be dropped due to shubha. That which leads to the negation of amputation is itself negated. Also, after amputation, the property is no longer inviolable with respect to the subject's right; if it remained inviolable, it would be permissible in itself, but amputation would be negated due to shubha. Instead, it becomes prohibited with respect to the Shari'ah's right, like carrion. There is no liability except that the lapse of inviolability is not apparent in the case of consumption because it is a separate act from theft, and there is no necessity in its regard. Similarly, shubha is considered in the cause, not others. The view of the mashhur (Abu Hanifa via Abu Yusuf) is that consumption is the completion of the purpose, so shubha is considered in it. Likewise, the lapse of inviolability appears with respect to liability because it is a necessary consequence of its lapse in the case of natural destruction, due to the lack of similarity.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah presents the ruling on the thief's liability after his hand has been amputated for the theft. The text states the general rule: if the stolen property is still in the thief's possession after the hand is cut, it must be returned to the owner; if it has been destroyed (whether by natural causes or by the thief's act), the thief is not liable for its value. This is the narration from Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa and is the famous (mashhur) position within the Hanafi school. However, Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that the thief is liable if he intentionally consumed/destroyed the property (istihlak). Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees completely, holding the thief liable in both cases (destruction and consumption) because the two rights (amputation and liability) have different causes and thus do not preclude each other. The Hanafi response is based on multiple proofs: first, the hadith 'La gharama 'ala al-sariq ba'da ma quti'at yaminahu' (No liability on the thief after his right hand is cut). Second, a rational proof: if the thief were required to pay compensation, he would become owner of the property from the moment he took it (by paying the value), creating a shubha (doubt) that would negate the amputation. Since amputation is a fixed hadd, anything that would negate it must be avoided. Therefore, liability is not imposed. Third, after amputation, the property loses its inviolability (isma) with respect to the victim's ownership right, becoming akin to carrion in terms of the Shari'ah's prohibition; no liability arises. The mashhur view (Abu Hanifa via Abu Yusuf) treats intentional consumption differently because it is a separate act completing the purpose of theft, but the shubha still precludes liability. This lesson illustrates how Hanafi usul balances multiple proofs and considers shubha in enforcing hadd penalties.

Key Terms
استهلاك (istihlak): Consumption or destruction of the stolen property by the thief's own act (e.g., using, eating, or selling).
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation or guarantee for lost or damaged property.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the famous Hanafi view (mashhur), what is the ruling regarding the thief's liability for the stolen property after his hand has been amputated?
A) The thief is liable for the value of the property in all cases, whether destroyed or not.
B) The thief is not liable at all, whether the property is destroyed by natural causes or consumed by him.
C) The thief is liable only if he consumed the property intentionally.
D) The thief is liable only if the property is still in his possession.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'La gharama 'ala al-sariq ba'da ma quti'at yaminuhu' establishes that after amputation, the thief is absolved from financial liability for destroyed property.

Ruling on Stolen Property After Amputation
Ruling and proof for liability after amputation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the thief's liability after amputation and explain the role of shubhah in negating guarantee.

Core Idea
After amputation, the thief is not liable for destroyed property because guarantee would create shubhah, nullifying the hadd.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a critical intersection of criminal law and property rights: after a thief's hand is cut, what happens to the stolen property? The Hanafi school has a well-known position that the thief does not guarantee destroyed property, based on a hadith and a nuanced argument about shubhah preventing the punishment. We will unpack the ruling, the proofs, and the intra- and inter-madhhab disputes.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its conditions
Identify the two scenarios: property still in hand versus destroyed. Note the obligation to return existing property and the non-liability for destroyed.
The ruling distinguishes between natural destruction and intentional consumption.
2
Identify the well-known position and the intra-Hanafi variant
The mashhur (well-known) position is from Abu Yusuf's narration: no guarantee in either case. Hasan ibn Ziyad narrates that Abu Hanifa held liability for intentional destruction.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree with the mashhur.
3
Examine the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that amputation and guarantee have different causes, so they do not prevent each other. He analogizes to destroying game in the Haram or drinking a dhimmi's wine.
The Shafi'i position is that the thief guarantees in both cases.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof from hadith and rationale
The hadith 'la gharam 'ala al-sariq ba'd ma quti'at yaminuh' (no liability after amputation) is cited. The rationale: requiring guarantee would retroactively make the thief owner, creating shubhah that voids the amputation.
The property loses inviolability as a servant's right and becomes like carrion.
5
Understand the role of shubhah
Shubhah is considered because guarantee would annul the hadd. For the mashhur position, intentional consumption is a completion of the theft, so shubhah applies similarly.
Shubhah is effective in the cause, not in separate acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قُطِعَ السَّارِقُ وَالْعَيْنُ قَائِمَةٌ فِي يَدِهِ رُدَّتْ إِلى صَاحِبِهَا لِبَقَائِهَا عَلَى مِلْكِهِ وَإِنْ كَانَتْ مُسْتَهْلَكَةٌ لَمْ يَضْمَنْ وَهذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ يَشْمُلُ الْهَلَاكَ وَالْإِسْتِهْلَاكَ وَهُوَ رِوَايَةُ أَبِى يُوسُفٌ عَنْ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَهُوَ الْمَشْهُورُ وَرَوَى الْحَسَنُ عَنْهُ أَنَّهُ يَضْمَنُ بِالْإِسْتِهْلَاكِ وَقَالَ الشَّافِعِيُّ يَضْمَنُ فِيهِمَا لِأَنَّهُمَا حَفَّانِ قَدْ اخْتَلَفَ سَبُهُمَا فَلَايَمْتَعَانِ فَالْقَطْعُ حَقُّ الشَّرْعِ وَسَبَهُ تَرْكُ الْإِنْتِهَاءِ عَمَّا نُهِيَ عَنْهُ وَالصَّمَانُ حَقُّ الْعَبْدِ وَسَبَبُهُ أَخْدُ الْمَالِ فَصَارَكَةِ سُتِهْلَاكِ صَيْدٍ مَمْلُوكِ فِي الْحَرَمِ اَوْشُرْبِ خَمْرٍ مَمْلُوْكَةِ لِلذَّمِّي وَلَنَا قَوْلُهُ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ لَا غَرَمَ عَلَى السَّارِقِ بَعْدَ مَا قُطِعَتْ يَمِيْنَهُ وَلَا وُجُوبَ الصَّمَانِ يُنَا فِي الْقَطْعَ لِأَنَّهُ يَتَمَلَكُهُ بِاَدَاءِ الصَّمَانِ مُسْتَنِدًا إِلى وَقْتِ الْاخْذِ فَتَبَيَّنَ أَنَّهُ وَرَدَ عَلَى مِلْكِهِ فَيَسْتَفِي الْقَطْعُ لِلشَّبْهَةِ وَمَا يُؤَدِّي إِلَى انْتِفَاتِهِ فَهُوَ الْمُنْتَفَى وَلَانَ الْمَحَلَّ لَا يَبْقَى مَعْصُوْمًا حَقًّا لِلْعَبْدِ إِذْ لَوْ بَقِيَ لَكَانَ مُبَاحًا فِي نَفْسِهِ فَيَنْتَفِي الْقَطْعُ لِلشُّبْهَةِ فَيَصِيرُ مُحَرَّمًا حَقًّا لِلشَّرْعِ كَالْمَيْتَةِ وَلَا ضَمَانَ فِيهِ إِلَّا أَنَّ الْعِصْمَةَ لَا يَظْهَرُ سُقُوطُهَا فِي حَقِ الْإِسْتِهْلَاكِ لِأَنَّهُ فِعْلٌ آخَرُ غَيْرُ السَّرْقَةِ وَلَا ضُرُورَةَ فِي حَقِهِ وَكَذَا الشُّبْهَةُ تُعْتَبَرُ فِيمَا هُوَ السَّبَبُ دُونَ غَيْرِهِ وَوَجْهُ الْمَشْهُورُ أَنَّ الْإِسْتِهْلَاكَ إِثْمَامُ الْمَقْصُودِ فَيُعْتَبَرُ الشَّبْهَةُ فِيْهِ وَكَذَايَظْهَرُ سُقُوطُ الْعِصْمَةِ فِي حَقِ الضَّمَانِ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ ضُرُورَاتِ سُقُوطِهَا فِى حَقِ الْهَلَاكِ لِانْتِفَاءِ الْمُمَاثَلَةِ
And when the thief is amputated and the object is still in his hand, it is returned to its owner because it remains his property. And if it is consumed/destroyed, he does not guarantee it. This general statement includes natural destruction and intentional consumption. This is the narration of Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa, and it is the well-known (mashhur) position. Al-Hasan narrated from him that he guarantees in case of intentional consumption. Al-Shafi'i said: He guarantees in both because they are two matters whose causes differ, so they do not prevent each other. Amputation is the right of the Shari'ah, its cause is refraining from what is prohibited; guarantee is the right of the servant, its cause is taking property. So it is like the destruction of owned game in the Haram or drinking owned wine of a dhimmi. And our proof is his statement (peace be upon him): 'There is no liability on the thief after his hand is cut off.' And there is no obligation of guarantee in amputation because he becomes owner of the property by paying the guarantee retroactively to the time of taking, so it becomes clear that he took his own property, so amputation is void due to shubhah. And what leads to its annulment is itself annulled. And because the property no longer remains inviolable as a right of the servant, for if it remained, it would be permissible in itself, so amputation would be negated due to shubhah, and it becomes prohibited as a right of the Shari'ah, like carrion, and there is no guarantee for it. However, the inviolability does not appear to be removed in the case of intentional consumption because it is a separate act from theft, and there is no necessity regarding it. And similarly, shubhah is considered for the cause, not for others. And the face of the well-known position is that intentional consumption is the completion of the purpose, so shubhah is considered in it. And similarly, the removal of inviolability appears regarding guarantee because it is a necessary consequence of its removal in the case of natural destruction, since there is no similarity.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hidayah: after a thief's hand is cut, if the stolen item remains, it is returned to the owner. If it is destroyed (naturally or intentionally), the thief is not liable according to the well-known Hanafi position (mashhur), narrated by Abu Yusuf from Abu Hanifa. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Hasan ibn Ziyad reports that Abu Hanifa holds the thief liable for intentional destruction. Imam al-Shafi'i holds liability in both cases. The author then provides the Hanafi proof: a hadith that there is no liability after amputation, and a rational argument that requiring guarantee would retroactively make the thief owner of the property, creating shubhah that prevents amputation. Since amputation is established, the guarantee must be negated. Furthermore, the stolen property loses its inviolability as a right of the servant and becomes inviolable only as a right of the Shari'ah (like carrion), which does not entail guarantee. For the mashhur position, intentional consumption is considered a completion of the theft, so shubhah applies, and the same reasoning holds for guarantee as for natural destruction.

Key Terms
قطعت (quti'a): Amputation of the thief's hand as a fixed punishment (hadd).
ضمان (daman): Financial liability or guarantee to compensate for the stolen property.
شبهة (shubhah): Doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of the hadd punishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) must pay for it.
B) does not pay for it.
C) pays only if the destruction was intentional.
D) pays half the value.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that after amputation, the thief is not liable for destroyed property because guarantee would introduce shubhah, negating the hadd.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Guarantee after Amputation for Multiple Thefts
Intra-Hanafi dispute on guarantee after amputation for one of multiple thefts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on financial liability when a thief is amputated for one of multiple thefts, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Hudud are subject to tadakhul: one amputation suffices for all thefts, but guarantee of property remains disputed.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the financial consequences of amputation for multiple thefts. While all agree that a single amputation covers the hadd penalty for all thefts, they differ on whether the thief must guarantee the stolen goods for which he was not amputated. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of tadakhul (mutual inclusion) in hudud and the role of litigation in establishing theft.

Guided Path
1
Identify the consensus and the dispute
Read the passage to see where all three imams agree (amputation suffices for all) and where they differ (guarantee of property).
The agreement occurs when all victims are present and litigate; the dispute when only some victims are present.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and their argument
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold the thief must guarantee all stolen goods except the one for which amputation was carried out. Their proof: litigation is required to establish each theft; absent victims' goods remain inviolable.
They focus on the procedural necessity of litigation (khurūm) for each separate theft.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and his argument
Abu Hanifa holds the thief guarantees nothing. His proof: hudud are based on mutual inclusion (tadakhul); the obligation is a single amputation for all thefts. Litigation is only a condition for appearance before the judge, not for the obligation itself.
He argues that the benefit of the amputation reverts to all victims, so it covers all.
4
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Consider a scenario where only one victim out of many litigates. According to Sahibayn, the thief must guarantee the property of the absent victims; according to Abu Hanifa, he does not.
This affects whether the thief remains financially liable after punishment.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text's structure (placing Abu Hanifa's argument last and using 'wa lahu') suggests his view is preferred. Later Hanafi fatwa works generally follow Abu Hanifa in this issue.
Relied-upon position: the thief is not liable for any stolen goods after amputation for one theft.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَ وَمَنْ سَرَقَ سَرْقَاتٍ فَقُطِعَ فِي إِحْدَاهَا فَهُوَ لِجَمِيعِهَا وَلَا يَضْمَنُ شَيْئًا عِنْدَابِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا يَضْمَنُ كُلَّهَا إِلَّا الَّتِي قَطَعَ لَهَا وَمَعْنَى الْمَسْأَلَةِ إِذَا حَضَرَاَحَدُهُمْ فَإِنْ حَضَرُوا جَمِيعًا وَ قُطِعَتْ يَدُهُ لِخُصُومَتِهِمْ لَا يَضْمَنُ شَيْئًا بِالْإِتِفَاقِ فِى السَّرْقَاتِ كُلِهَا لَهُمَا اَنَّ الْحَاضِرَ لَيْسَ بِنَائِبِ عَنِ الْغَائِبِ وَلَابُدَّ مِنَ الْخُصُومَةِ لِتَظْهَرَ السَّرْقَةُ فَلَمْ تَظْهَرِ السَّرْقَةُ مِنَ الْغَائِبِينَ فَلَمْ يَقَعِ الْقَطْعُ لَهَا فَبَقِيَتْ اَمْوَالُهُمْ مَعْصُومَةً وَلَهُ أَنَّ الْوَاجِبَ بِالْكُلَّ قَطْعُ وَاحِدٍ حَق اللهِ تَعَالَى لِاَنَّ مَبْنَى الْحُدُودِ عَلَى التَّدَاخُلِ وَالْخُصُومَةُ شَرْطُ الظُّهُورِ عِنْدَ الْقَاضِي أَمَّا الْوُجُوبُ بِالْجَنَايَةِ فَإِذَا اسْتَوْفِى فَالْمُسْتَوْفِى كُلُّ الْوَاجِبِ اَلَاتَرى أَنَّهُ يَرْجِعُ نَفْعُهُ إِلَى الْكُلِ فَيَقَعُ عَنِ الْكُلِّ وَعَلَى هَذَا الْخِلَافِ إذَا كَانَتِ النَّصَبُ كُلَّهَا لِوَاحِدٍ فَخَاصَمَ فِي الْبَعْضِ
He said: And whoever commits multiple thefts and then is amputated for one of them, it suffices for all, and he does not guarantee anything according to Abu Hanifa. But they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He guarantees all except the one for which he was amputated. The meaning of the matter: if one of them (the victims) is present (to litigate); if all are present and his hand is cut due to their litigation, then he does not guarantee anything by agreement for all thefts. Their (Sahibayn's) argument: The present one does not represent the absent ones, and litigation is necessary to establish the theft, so theft from the absent ones is not established, so amputation does not occur for them, so their property remains inviolable. And his (Abu Hanifa's) argument: The obligation for all is one amputation as a right of Allah Most High, because hudud are based on mutual inclusion. Litigation is a condition for appearance before the judge, but as for obligation due to the crime, when it is fulfilled, what is fulfilled is all that is owed. Do you not see that its benefit reverts to all, so it occurs for all. And the same dispute applies if all the goods belong to one person and he litigates for some of them.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario of a thief who commits multiple thefts and then his hand is cut for one of them. The base ruling (from Quduri) is unanimously agreed: the amputation serves as a punishment for all thefts. The dispute is about financial liability for the other stolen goods. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the thief does not guarantee any of the stolen property. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that he must guarantee all the other stolen goods except the one for which he was amputated. The text explains the subtle condition: if all victims are present and litigate, then by agreement no guarantee is due because the amputation was established through litigation for all. The difference arises when only one victim is present. Sahibayn argue that the absent victims' thefts are not established by the present one's litigation, so their goods remain inviolable and the thief must guarantee them. Abu Hanifa argues that the obligation (of hadd) for all thefts is a single amputation, and hudud are subject to mutual inclusion (tadakhul). Litigation is only a condition for the judge to know, not for the obligation itself. Once the amputation is executed, it fulfills all obligations, and its benefit reverts to all victims, so it covers all. The same dispute applies if all stolen goods belong to one person and he litigates only for some.

Key Terms
سرقات (sariqāt): Multiple distinct acts of theft committed by the same person.
تداخل (tadākhul): Mutual inclusion of hudud punishments when multiple crimes are committed before any execution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), if a thief steals from many people and only one victim litigates, leading to amputation, what is the ruling on the other stolen goods?
A) All stolen goods are covered by the amputation and no guarantee is due.
B) The thief guarantees all stolen goods except the one for which he was amputated.
C) The thief guarantees all stolen goods including the one for which he was amputated.
D) The thief guarantees only the goods of the litigating victim.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, hudud are subject to tadakhul, so one amputation suffices for multiple thefts; the relied-upon position is that no financial guarantee is due to any victim, though Sahibayn differ.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Thief Tears Cloth – Is the Hand Cut?
Ruling on amputation when thief tears cloth before removal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a thief's tear creating ownership prevents hadd, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
A thief's tear inside the house does not create sufficient doubt to drop the hadd; the hand is cut.

In this lesson we examine a nuanced case within the Hanafi school: a thief tears a valuable cloth into two halves while still in the owner's house, then takes the pieces out. The majority of the school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that amputation is still required, but a narration from Abu Yusuf suggests otherwise. We will dissect the reasoning behind each position and understand the precise conditions that govern the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The thief's act: tearing the cloth into two halves inside the house, then removing it. The default ruling from the matn: hand is cut, assuming the nisāb (10 dirhams) is met.
The matn's position is the starting point.
2
Analyze Abu Yusuf's dissenting argument
Abu Yusuf argues that the significant tear (kharq fāḥish) creates a cause of ownership: the thief is liable for the value, and by paying it he becomes owner, similar to a buyer with a right of option (khiyār). Thus, his taking is not pure theft and the hadd drops.
This introduces the concept of shubha (doubt) based on potential ownership.
3
Examine the majority's rebuttal
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond that the act of taking is a cause for liability (ḍamān), not ownership. Ownership arises only as a necessity to avoid double compensation, and such derivative ownership does not create a shubha that can prevent hadd.
They analogize to the case of the seller stealing the sold item, where sale is meant for ownership.
4
Distinguish the agreed case
If the owner chooses to claim the full value and leave the cloth with the thief, then by agreement the hand is not cut. Here the thief becomes owner from the time of taking, creating a genuine shubha.
This is the practical consequence: the owner's choice determines the application of the dispute.
5
Apply the distinction between significant and minor tear
All the above applies only when the tear is significant (fāḥish). If the tear is minor (yasīr), the hand is cut by agreement because no cause of ownership arises.
The degree of damage is legally relevant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بَابُ مَا يُحْدِثُ السَّارِقُ فِي السَّرْقَةِ وَ مَنْ سَرَقَ ثَوْبًا فَشَقَّهُ فِى الدَّارِ بِنِصْفَيْنِ ثُمَّ أَخْرَجَهُ وَهُوَ يُسَاوِي عَشْرَةَ دَرَاهِمَ قُطِعَ وَعَنْ أَبِى يُوسُفَ أَنَّهُ لَا يُقْطَعُ لِأَنَّ لَهُ فِيهِ سَبَبَ المِلْكِ وَهُوَ الْخَرْقَ الْفَاحِشُ فَإِنَّهُ يُوجِبُ الْقِيْمَةَ وَتَمَلُكَ الْمَضْمُونِ وَصَارَ كَالْمُشْتَرِي إِذَا سَرَقَ مَبِيعًا فِيهِ خِيَارٌ لِلْبَائِعِ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّ الْأَخْذَ وُضِعَ سَبَبًا لِلصَّمَانِ لَا لِلْمِلْكِ وَإِنَّمَا الْمِلْكَ يَثْبُتُ ضُرُورَةَ أَدَاءِ الصَّمَانِ كَيْلَا يَجْتَمِعَ الْبَدَلَانِ فِي مِلْكِ وَاحِدٍ وَمِثْلُهُ لَا يُوْرِثُ الشُّبُهَةَ كَنَفْسِ الْأَخْذِ وَكَمَا إِذَا سَرَقَ الْبَائِعُ مَبِيعًا بَاعَهُ بِخِلَافِ مَا ذُكِرَ لِأَنَّ الْبَيْعَ مَوْضُوعَ لِإِفَادَةِ الْمِلْكِ وَهَذَا الْخِلَافُ فِيمَا إِذَا اخْتَارَ تَضْمِينَ النُّقْصَانِ وَأَخَذَ الثَّوْبَ فَإِنِ اخْتَارَ تَضْمِينَ الْقِيْمَةِ وَتَرَكَ الثَّوْبَ عَلَيْهِ لَا يُقْطَعُ بِالْاِتِّفَاقِ لِأَنَّهُ مِلْكُهُ مُسْتَبِدًا إِلَى وَقْتِ الْأَخْذِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا مَلَكَهُ بِالْهِبَةِ فَأَوْرَثَ شُبْهَةً وَهَذَا كُلُّهُ إِذَا كَانَ النُّقْصَانُ فَاحِشًا فَإِنْ كَانَ يَسِيرًا يُقْطَعُ بِالْاتِّفَاقِ لِانْعِدَامِ سَبَبِ الْمِلْكِ إِذْ لَيْسَ لَهُ اخْتِيَارُ تَضْمِينِ كُلِّ الْقِيْمَةِ
Chapter on what the thief does to the stolen property. Whoever steals a cloth and tears it in the house into two halves, then takes it out while it is worth ten dirhams, his hand is cut. And from Abu Yusuf that it is not cut because he has a means of ownership in it, which is the significant tear, for it necessitates the value (damān) and he owns what is guaranteed, and it becomes like a buyer who steals a sold item that has a right of option for the seller. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): Taking (theft) is established as a cause for liability, not for ownership. Ownership only arises necessarily for payment of the value so that two compensations do not combine in one ownership, and such does not create doubt (shubha) like the taking itself. And likewise if the seller steals a sold item he sold, contrary to what was mentioned, because the sale is established for ownership. And this difference is when he chooses to guarantee the defect and take the cloth. But if he chooses to guarantee the full value and leaves the cloth with him, he is not cut by agreement, because it is his property from the time of taking, so it is like when he owned it by gift, creating doubt. All this when the defect is significant. If it is minor, he is cut by agreement due to the absence of a cause of ownership, since he does not have the option to guarantee the full value.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a thief who tears a cloth into two halves while still inside the owner's house, then takes the torn pieces out. The cloth's value is at least ten dirhams (nisāb). The default ruling (from the matn) is that the hand is cut. However, a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf states that the hand is not cut, because the significant tear (kharq fāḥish) gives the thief a cause of ownership: by tearing, he is liable for the value, and by paying that value he becomes owner of the cloth. Thus, his taking becomes similar to a buyer who steals an item he had purchased but for which the seller still has a right of option (khiyār). The Sahibayn (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) respond that the act of taking (akhdh) is a cause for liability (ḍamān), not ownership. Ownership only arises as a necessity for paying the value, to avoid combining two compensations (the cloth itself and its value) in one ownership. Such a necessity does not create a doubt (shubha) that would prevent the hadd. The difference is when the owner chooses to claim the cloth and compensation for the defect; but if the owner chooses to claim the full value and leave the cloth with the thief, then by agreement the hand is not cut, because the thief becomes owner from the time of taking (due to the option given to the owner), which creates a doubt. All this applies when the tear is significant (fāḥish). If the tear is minor (yasīr), the hand is cut by agreement because there is no cause of ownership.

Key Terms
خَرْق فَاحِش (kharq fāḥish): A significant tear that substantially reduces the value of the cloth, such that it creates an obligation to compensate for the defect.
شُبْهَة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd punishment, here arising from a claim of ownership or right in the stolen property.
تَضْمِين (taḍmīn): Imposition of liability (damān) to pay compensation for the value or defect of the stolen property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (muftā bihī) position, if a thief tears a valuable cloth into two halves inside the house before taking it out, is his hand amputated?
A) Yes, because the act of taking remains unlawful.
B) No, because the tear gives him ownership.
C) Only if the owner chooses to claim the cloth.
D) Only if the tear is minor.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute hinges on whether a significant tear creates a shubha; the relied-upon position is that the hadd remains.
