Multiple Thefts and Hand-Cutting: Liability Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability after multiple thefts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability after hand-cutting for multiple thefts and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
One hand-cut covers all thefts, but Sahibayn require liability for absent victims.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling within the Hanafi school: if a thief commits several thefts and his hand is cut for only one of them, does he owe compensation for the others? The matn states the relied-upon position that the cut suffices for all, but the commentaries reveal a disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how hudud punishments interact with financial rights (daman).

Guided Path
1
Identify the matn ruling
Read the matn: he who commits several thefts and is cut for one — that cut is for all and he does not owe compensation. This is the position that will be compared.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Note that the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they hold that if only one victim appears to claim, the cut applies only to that victim, and the thief must compensate the absent victims.
The dispute hinges on whether the punishment covers all thefts automatically or only those brought before the qadi.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof (tadakhul)
Abu Hanifa argues that hudud penalties overlap (tadakhul al-hudud). Since the hadd is a right of Allah, one punishment satisfies all thefts, and financial liability is also extinguished because the crime is fully expiated.
This is based on the principle that multiple hadd punishments for similar crimes merge into one.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the absent victims have a financial right (daman) that is not automatically cancelled by a punishment imposed for a different theft. Their property rights remain protected until they themselves claim or the cut is explicitly for their theft.
They prioritize the right of the individual over the merging of punishments.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The matn and the later commentary (Ashraf al-Hidayah) adopt Abu Hanifa's view as the relied-upon position. However, the Sahibayn's view is also preserved and considered valid.
In practice, the mufti would follow the matn, but the dispute shows the school's internal reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَمَنْ سَرَقَ سَرْقَاتٍ فَقُطِعَ فِي إِحْدَهَا فَهُوَ لِجَمِيعِهَا وَلَا يَضْمَنُ
He said: Whoever commits several thefts and his hand is cut for one of them, that cut is for all of them and he is not liable (for the others).
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the core ruling on multiple thefts and an intra-Hanafi dispute. The matn states the relied-upon position: when a thief's hand is cut for one theft, it suffices for all, and he does not owe compensation for the other thefts. However, the commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains that there is a disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Sahibayn argue that if only one victim of multiple thefts appears to claim, the thief is liable for the other victims' property because the hand-cutting was not on behalf of those absent; the thief owes them compensation. Abu Hanifa argues that the hand-cutting is a single punishment for all thefts because hudud (penalties) overlap (تداخل), and the punishment serves as a deterrent for all. The commentary follows the order of the original text, first stating the ruling, then presenting the dispute with proofs for each side.

Key Terms
تداخل الحدود (tadakhul al-hudud): The principle that when multiple hadd punishments are due, they overlap and one punishment suffices for all.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate the value of stolen property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (matn) in the Hanafi school, if a thief commits multiple thefts and his hand is cut for one of them, what is the ruling on his liability for the other thefts?
A) He must compensate all other victims.
B) He must compensate only if the other victims appear in court.
C) He is not liable for any other thefts.
D) He is liable only if the stolen goods exceed ten dirhams each.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi holds that hand-cutting for one theft extinguishes liability for all thefts due to the overlap of hudud, though the Sahibayn's view protects absent victims' rights.

Thief Tears Stolen Cloth: When Does a Tear Prevent Hand-Cutting?
Ruling on hand-cutting when thief tears cloth before removal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hand-cutting when a thief tears stolen cloth inside the house and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Muhammad from Abu Yusuf, applying the concept of shubha.

Core Idea
An excessive tear in stolen property does not create ownership shubha by itself; only when the owner claims full value does shubha arise by agreement.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the application of hadd (hand-cutting) when a thief alters stolen property before removal. The key question is whether the tear creates a cause of ownership (sabab al-milk) that generates shubha, thus blocking the punishment. We will trace the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad versus a transmission from Abu Yusuf, and clarify when the ruling changes based on the owner's choice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
The thief steals a cloth, tears it into two halves inside the house, then takes it out. The matn rules that the hand is cut. Abu Yusuf (one transmission) says it is not cut.
The dispute hinges on whether the tear creates a cause of ownership.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's argument
The excessive tear obligates the thief to pay the full value, making him owner of the cloth by guaranty (daman). This creates a shubha similar to a buyer under khiyar.
Abu Yusuf analogizes to a buyer who steals an item with a seller's khiyar.
3
Analyze the majority response
Taking (akhdh) is for guaranty, not ownership. Ownership arises only after payment to avoid combining two substitutes. The act of taking itself does not create shubha, similar to a seller stealing back a defective item.
The majority distinguish between cause for guaranty and cause for ownership.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The dispute applies only when the owner chooses to take the cloth and claim compensation for the defect. If the owner claims full value and leaves the cloth, all agree no cut, as ownership becomes complete.
The practical consequence depends on the owner's choice.
5
Distinguish excessive vs. minor tear
If the tear is minor (yasir), the thief has no option to pay full value, so no cause of ownership and unanimous cut.
Excessive tear gives the owner the choice between claiming full value or taking the cloth with compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَ مَنْ سَرَقَ ثَوْ بَافَشَقَّهُ فِى الدَّارِ بِنِصْفَيْنِ ثُمَّ أَخْرَجَهُ وَهُوَيُسَاوِي عَشْرَةَ دَرَاهِمَ قُطِعَ وَعَنْ أَبِى يُوسُفٌ أَنَّهُ لَا يُقْطَعُ لَا لَهُ فِيهِ سَبَبُ المِلْكِ وَهُوَ الْخَرْقَ الْفَاحِشُ فَإِنَّهُ يُوجِبُ الْقِيْمَةَ وَتَمَلُكَ الْمَضْمُونِ وَصَارَ كَالْمَشْتَرِى إِذَا سَرَقَ مَبِيْعَافِيهِ خِيَارٌ لِلْبَائِعَ وَلَهُمَا أَنَّ الْأَخْذَ وُضِعَ سَبَبًا لِلصَّمَانِ لَا لِلْمِلْكِ وَإِنَّمَا الْمِلْكَ يَثْبُتُ ضُرُورَةَ أَدَاءِ الصَّمَانِ كَيْلَايَجْتَمِعَ الْبَدَلَانِ فِي مِلْكِ وَاحِدٍ وَمِثْلُهُ لَا يُوْرِثُ الشُّبُهَةَ كَنَفْسِ الْأَحْدِ وَكَمَا إِذَا سَرَقَ الْبَائِعُ مُعِيْبًا بَاعَهُ بِخِلَافِ مَا ذُكِرَلَانَّ الْبَيْعَ مَوْضُوعَ لِإِفَادَةِ الْمِلْكِ وَهَذَا الْخِلَافُ فِيمَا إِذَا اختارَ تَضْمِينَ النُّقْصَانِ وَاَخَذَ الثّوبِ فَإِنِ اخْتَارَ تَضْمِينَ الْقِيْمَةِ وَتَرَكَ التَّوْبَ عَلَيْهِ لَا يُقْطَعُ بِالْاِتِّفَاقِ لِأَنَّهُ مِلْكُهُ مُسْتَبَدًا إِلَى وَقْتِ الْأَخْذِ فَصَارَ كَمَا إِذَا مَلَكَهُ بِالْهِبَةِ فَأَوْرَثَ شُبُهَةٌ وَهَذَا كُلُّهُ إِذَا كَانَ النُّقْصَانُ فَاحِشًا فَإِنْ كَانَ يَسِيرًا يُقْطَعُ بِالْاتِفَاقِ لِانْعِدَامِ سَبَبِ الْمِلْكِ اذْلَيْسَ لَهُ اخْتِيَارُ تَضْمِيْنِ كُلَّ الْقِيْمَةِ
And whoever steals a cloth and tears it into two halves inside the house, then takes it out while it is worth ten dirhams, his hand is cut. And from Abu Yusuf (a transmission) that his hand is not cut because he has a cause of ownership in it, and that is the excessive tear, for it necessitates the value and he owns the guaranteed (object), and it becomes like the buyer when he steals a sold item in which the seller has a Khiyar. And for them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): the taking is placed as a cause for guaranty, not for ownership; ownership is established only out of necessity for payment of the guaranty so that two substitutes do not combine in one ownership. And the like of it does not cause doubt, like the taking itself, and like when the seller steals a defective item he sold; contrary to what was mentioned, because sale is placed for effecting ownership. And this dispute is when he chooses compensation for the defect and takes the cloth. If he chooses compensation for the value and leaves the cloth, he is not cut by agreement, because the cloth is his property absolutely up to the time of taking, so it becomes like when he owned it by gift, thus causing doubt. And all this is when the defect is excessive. If it is minor, he is cut by agreement because there is no cause of ownership, since he does not have the choice to compensate the full value.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute. It addresses a specific scenario: a thief steals a cloth, tears it into two pieces while still inside the owner's house, then takes the torn pieces out. The value is at least ten dirhams. The matn states the hand is cut (Imam Abu Hanifa's view, supported by Imam Muhammad). However, a transmission from Abu Yusuf says the hand is not cut. The reasoning hinges on whether the tear gives the thief a cause of ownership. Abu Yusuf argues that the excessive tear (fahish) obligates the thief to pay the full value (qima), thus he becomes owner of the cloth by way of guaranty (daman). This creates a shubha (doubt) that prevents the hadd. He analogizes to a buyer who steals an item he purchased but over which the seller has a khiyar (option). In that case, the buyer has a cause of ownership via the contract, so no cut. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond: taking (akhdh) is a cause for guaranty, not ownership; ownership arises later only out of necessity to avoid having both the cloth and its value in the same ownership. This does not create shubha—similar to the act of taking itself or the case of a seller stealing back a defective item he sold (where khiyar does not create ownership in the seller before return). The dispute is when the owner chooses to take the cloth and claim compensation for the defect (naqsan). If instead the owner chooses to claim the full value and leave the cloth with the thief, then by agreement there is no cut because the thief becomes owner of the cloth from the time of taking (via payment of value), similar to a gift, creating shubha. Finally, if the tear is minor (yasir), the thief has no cause of ownership because he cannot choose to pay the full value, so hand-cutting is unanimous.

Key Terms
شق فاحش (shaqq fahish): An excessive tear in the cloth that reduces its value significantly, obligating the full value as compensation.
شبهة (shubha): A legal doubt that prevents the application of hadd punishment.
ضمان (daman): Guaranty; liability to pay compensation for property destroyed or damaged by the thief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school (matn), if a thief steals a cloth worth ten dirhams, tears it into two halves inside the house, and then takes it out, what is the ruling?
A) His hand is not cut because the tear creates ownership via guaranty.
B) His hand is cut because the tear is a cause for guaranty, not ownership.
C) His hand is not cut if the tear is minor.
D) His hand is cut only if he also destroys other property.

Key Takeaway
An excessive tear in stolen cloth does not prevent hand-cutting unless the owner claims full value, creating shubha by agreement; minor tears do not affect the punishment.

Tearing the Cloth Inside the House: Hadd or No Hadd?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hadd for tearing cloth before removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf) on amputation when a thief tears cloth before removal, and explain the role of shubhah in preventing hadd.

Core Idea
A cause of ownership creates shubhah that prevents hadd – but when does that cause arise?

In Hanafi fiqh, the fixed penalty (hadd) for theft is lifted if any legal doubt (shubhah) surrounds the ownership of the stolen item. This lesson examines a specific intra-Hanafi dispute: a thief steals a cloth, tears it into two halves inside the house, then removes it. Does the tearing – which makes him liable for compensation – create a shubhah that bars amputation? Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad say no; Imam Abu Yusuf says yes. Let's unpack their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
Read the passage and identify the key facts: cloth stolen, torn inside the house into two halves, then removed. The thief becomes liable for the value due to the tearing.
The act of tearing is inside the hirz, so removal occurs after the tear.
2
Identify the source of dispute
Note that the dispute centers on whether the combination of liability and taking gives the thief a 'cause of ownership' (sabab al-milkiyya) that creates shubhah.
Abu Yusuf sees the liability as a cause of ownership; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not.
3
Analyze each position's dalil
Abu Hanifa & Muhammad: mere taking is a cause for liability (ḍamān), not ownership. Ownership only arises after paying compensation. Therefore, no shubhah at the time of taking. Abu Yusuf: the liability itself gives a right to own the cloth by paying, so a shubhah exists from the moment of taking.
This is a pure rational (ʿaqlī) argument about the legal effect of liability.
4
Determine the practical consequence
If the owner accepts the damaged cloth and takes back the compensation, Abu Hanifa & Muhammad cut the hand, Abu Yusuf does not. If the owner accepts compensation and leaves the cloth with the thief, all agree no cut (because ownership is then established).
The thamarah al-khilaf is only in the first scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ سَرَقَ ثَوْبَافَشَقَّهُ فِي الدَّارِ بِنِصْفَيْنِ ثُمَّ أَخْرَجَهُ وَهُوَ يُسَاوِى عَشَرَة .. الخ
And whoever steals a cloth and tears it into two halves in the house, then takes it out, while its value is ten...
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where a thief steals a cloth, tears it into two pieces inside the house, then removes it. The act of tearing makes him liable for the value, and subsequently, by compensating (or agreeing to compensate), he becomes owner of the cloth. The text compares this to a buyer who steals an item for which the seller retained an option (khiyar). In both cases, a cause of ownership exists, creating doubt (shubha) that prevents amputation. Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad argue that mere taking is a cause for liability, not ownership; ownership only arises upon payment of compensation. Therefore, the taking itself does not create doubt, and the hand should be cut. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, holding that the combination of taking and the resulting liability creates a shubha, so the hand is not cut. This ikhtilaf occurs only when the owner agrees to accept the damage compensation and take back the cloth. If the owner instead accepts compensation and leaves the cloth with the thief, all agree that the hand is not cut, because the thief becomes owner from the moment of taking (via the compensation). The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated but the weight of argument suggests the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms
شَقَّ (shaqqa): To tear or split into pieces, here specifically tearing a cloth into two halves inside the house before removal.
ضَمَان (daman): Liability for compensation; here the thief becomes liable for the value of the cloth due to damaging it.
شُبْهَة (shubhah): A legal doubt that prevents the application of the fixed penalty (hadd).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, if a thief tears a cloth into two halves inside the house before taking it out, what is the ruling on amputation?
A) Hand is cut because no shubha exists.
B) Hand is not cut because ownership is established.
C) Hand is cut only if the owner demands compensation.
D) Hand is not cut because the cloth was not removed from the hirz.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on whether taking with liability creates shubhah highlights the critical role of the timing of ownership in hadd; the hand is cut only when no genuine claim to ownership exists at the moment of taking.

No Amputation for Theft of Meat After Slaughter Inside Hirz
Ruling on amputation for stealing a sheep and slaughtering it before taking out.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that amputation is not applied when a stolen animal is slaughtered before removal, and explain that the theft is then considered as of meat.

Core Idea
Slaughtering a stolen sheep inside the hirz transforms the theft to meat, and there is no amputation for meat.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario in Islamic criminal law (hudud): a thief who steals a sheep, slaughters it while still inside the owner's property (hirz), and then takes the meat out. The Hanafi school rules that the hand is not cut because the stolen item is now meat, which is not subject to amputation. This ruling highlights an important principle: the continuity of the theft from the place of protection is interrupted by the transformation of the stolen property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
State that the act is theft of a sheep followed by slaughter before removal. The ruling is that the hadd (amputation) is not applied.
The ruling is specific to the sequence: slaughter before removal.
2
Pinpoint the Critical Action: Slaughter Before Removal
Note that the thief slaughters the sheep while it is still in the hirz (place of protection). This action changes the stolen item from a living animal to meat.
If the thief had taken the sheep out alive, amputation might apply if the nisab is met.
3
Understand the Legal Reasoning
The reasoning given is 'because the theft was completed on meat, and there is no amputation for meat.' The moment of completion (qabd) occurs on meat, which is not subject to hadd.
This reasoning is based on Hanafi principles that meat is not considered a high-value item for hadd purposes.
4
Compare with Related Cases
Contrast this with a case where the thief takes the sheep out alive: then the theft is of a live animal, and if its value reaches the nisab, amputation applies. Also, if the thief slaughters after removal, the theft was completed on the live animal before slaughter, so amputation applies (assuming nisab).
The key distinction is the order: slaughter before removal vs. after removal.
5
Note the Scope of the Ruling
This ruling is part of the Hanafi school's doctrine on hudud. It is accepted by all major schools (muttafaq alayh) concerning the general principle that meat is not subject to amputation.
Other schools may have different nisab conditions, but the core ruling is consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ سَرَقَ شَاةً فَذَبَحَهَا ثُمَّ اَخْرَجَهَا لَمْ يُقْطَعُ لأنَّ السَّرْقَةَ تَمَّتْ عَلَى اللَّحْمِ وَلَا قَطْعَ فِيهِ
And if someone steals a sheep and slaughters it, then takes it out, his hand is not cut because the theft was completed on meat, and there is no amputation for it.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This is a clear ruling: a thief who slaughters a stolen sheep before removing it from the place of protection (hirz) avoids amputation because the act of slaughtering changes the stolen item from a living animal (subject to hadd if nisab is met) to meat, for which there is no amputation. The theft is effectively of meat, and the hadd is not applicable. This ruling assumes the sheep reached the nisab for amputation. The hand is not cut regardless of the method of slaughter.

Key Terms
ذَبَحَ (dhabaha): To slaughter an animal, here transforming the stolen sheep into meat.
لَحْم (lahm): Meat; the stolen item after slaughter, for which there is no amputation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a thief steals a sheep, slaughters it inside the house, and then takes the meat out, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) Hand is cut because the sheep was stolen.
B) Hand is not cut because the theft was completed on meat.
C) Hand is cut only if the slaughtering was done outside.
D) Hand is not cut only if the value is less than nisab.

Key Takeaway
Amputation is not imposed for theft of meat, even if the animal was stolen alive but slaughtered before removal.

Ruling on Amputation and Return of Property When Thief Transforms Stolen Gold/Silver into Coins
Intra-Hanafi dispute on hudud and property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding amputation (hadd) and return of property when a thief transforms stolen gold or silver into coins, and identify the practical consequence of each position.

Core Idea
Transforming stolen metal into coins does not erase the original owner's right according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn view the transformation as a valuable craft that transfers ownership.

This lesson examines a nuanced case from Hanafi hudud law: a thief steals gold or silver that meets the threshold for amputation, then melts it down and mints it into dirhams or dinars. Does the amputation still apply? Who owns the coins? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, differ on both questions. Their disagreement hinges on whether the transformation constitutes a 'valuable craft' (sun'ah mutaqawwimah) that changes ownership of the substance.

Guided Path
1
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Identify the two rulings: amputation (hadd) and return of coins. Write them down as a single clear position.
Abu Hanifa: hand cut, coins returned.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's position
State what Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold regarding the return of coins and the hadd. Note the two opinions on hadd within their view.
Sahibayn: coins not returned; hadd either obligatory (if it became a new thing) or not (if thief owned the metal).
3
Follow the analogy to 'asab
The Sahibayn base their ruling on qiyas to the case of dyeing cloth ('asab). Understand that the craft (sun'ah mutaqawwimah) in dyeing gives the dyer a right, and similarly the thief's craft gives him ownership.
In dyeing, the cloth changes color; in coining, the metal is reshaped but remains the same substance.
4
Analyze the implication for hadd
Examine how the dispute over ownership affects the application of amputation. Abu Hanifa's view keeps hadd straightforward; the Sahibayn's view introduces uncertainty.
If thief owns the metal before amputation, hadd might not apply because it is his own property.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, coins returned to owner; under Sahibayn, thief keeps coins. The hadd might or might not be applied under Sahibayn.
This shows how a difference in legal reasoning directly affects both criminal punishment and property rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ سَرَقَ ذَهَبًا أَوْ فِضَّةً يَجِبُ فِيهِ الْقَطْعُ فَصَنَعَهُ دَرَاهِمَ أَوْ دَنَانِيرَ قُطِعَ فِيهِ وَيُرَدَّ الدَّرَاهِمُ وَالدَّنَانِيرُ إِلَى الْمَسْرُوقِ مِنْهُ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَقَالَا لَا سَبِيلَ لِلْمَسْرُوقِ مِنْهُ عَلَيْهِمَا وَأَصْلُهُ فِي الْعَصَبِ فَهَذِهِ صُنْعَةٌ مُتَقَوَّمَةٌ عِنْدَهُمَا خِلَافًا لَهُ ثُمَّ وُجُوبُ الْحَدِّ لَا يُشْكَلُ عَلَى قَوْلِهِ لِأَنَّهُ لَا يَمْلِكُهُ وَقِيلَ عَلَى قَوْلِهِمَا لَا يَجِبُ لِأَنَّهُ مَلَكَهُ قَبْلَ الْقَطْعِ وَقِيلَ يَجِبُ لِأَنَّهُ صَارَ بِالصُّنْعَةِ شَيْئًا آخَرَ فَلَمْ يَمْلِكْ عَيْنَهُ
And whoever steals gold or silver for which amputation is obligatory, then makes it into dirhams or dinars, he is amputated for it, and the dirhams and dinars are returned to the one from whom they were stolen. This is according to Abu Hanifah. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: The one from whom it is stolen has no recourse to them. Its origin is in the case of 'asab (a dye plant). So this is a valuable craft according to them, contrary to him. Then the obligation of the hadd is not problematic according to his saying because he does not own it. And it is said that according to them it is not obligatory because he owned it before the amputation. And it is said it is obligatory because it became something else by the craft, so he does not own the same thing.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage deals with a thief who steals gold or silver that meets the nisab for amputation, and then transforms it into coins (dirhams or dinars). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the thief's hand is cut, and the coins are returned to the original owner. According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the original owner has no claim to the coins; instead, the thief becomes owner of the coins by virtue of the transformation (a valuable craft). The root of this ruling is analogized to the case of 'asab (a plant used for dyeing cloth). The text then discusses the implication for hadd: on Abu Hanifa's view, hadd applies without issue because the thief never owned the gold/silver. On the Sahibayn's view, there is a question: some say hadd is not obligatory because the thief owned the metal before amputation (due to transformation), while others say hadd is obligatory because the transformation made it something else, so he does not own the original 'ayn. The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated, but Abu Hanifa's view is straightforward.

Key Terms
صُنْعَة مُتَقَوِّمَة (sun'ah mutaqawwimah): A craft or manufacturing that adds value to the property, making it compensable.
عَصَب ('asab): A plant used for dyeing cloth; a standard case in Hanafi fiqh for analogy on transformation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis of the Sahibayn's ruling that the thief keeps the coins?
A) The thief's skill gives him ownership.
B) The gold becomes a different substance.
C) The owner abandoned it.
D) The value is less than nisab.

Key Takeaway
The transformation of stolen property does not necessarily erase the original owner's right; the Hanafi school debates whether a valuable craft can transfer ownership, affecting both criminal and civil consequences.

Whoever steals a cloak and dyes it red
Ruling on amputation and return of dyed cloth with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a stolen dyed cloth, explain the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and apply the distinction between formal and substantive rights.

Core Idea
A thief who dyes a stolen cloth keeps it; the owner's right is only formal, not substantive.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in the law of theft: what happens when a thief alters the stolen property by dyeing it? The Hanafi school is divided: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that the dyed cloth remains with the thief, while Muhammad argues it must be returned. Understanding the underlying principle of formal (surah) versus substantive (ma'nan) rights is critical to grasping the preferred Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: the thief is amputated, the cloth is not taken, and no value is guaranteed.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam Muhammad holds that the cloth must be taken from the thief and returned to the owner, who pays for the increase from the dye.
Muhammad uses qiyas to ghasb.
3
Analyze the opposing proof
Muhammad analogizes to ghasb: in ghasb, the cloth is the basis and the dye is subsidiary, so the owner gets the cloth and compensates for the dye.
His 'illa is that the cloth is the original basis (asl) and the dye is subsidiary (tabi').
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf distinguish theft from ghasb. In theft, the owner's right is only formal (surah) because the thief is not liable if the cloth perishes; the dye is substantial in both form and meaning, so the balance tips to the thief.
The key distinction is that in ghasb, both parties have substantive rights, so they are equal and owner prevails.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under the preferred view, the owner cannot demand the dyed cloth back; under Muhammad's view, he can but must pay for the dye.
This outcome is called thamarah al-khilaf.

فَإِنْ سَرَقَ قُبًا فَصَبَغَهُ أَحْمَرَ قُطِعَ وَلَمْ يُؤْخَذْ مِنْهُ الْقُبُ وَلَمْ يَضْمَنْ قِيمَةَ الثَّوْبِ وَهَذَا عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِي يُوسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ يُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ الثَّوْبُ وَيُعْطَى مَا زَادَ الصَّبْغُ فِيهِ اعْتِبَارًا بِالْعَصَبِ وَالْجَامِعُ بَيْنَهُمَا كَوْنُ الثَّوْبِ أَصْلًا قَائِمًا وَكَوْنُ الصَّبْغِ تَابِعًا وَلَهُمَا أَنَّ الصَّبْغَ قَائِمٌ صُورَةً وَمَعْنَى حَتَّى لَوْ أَرَادَ أَخْذَهُ مَصْبُوغًا يَضْمَنُ مَا زَادَ الصَّبْغُ فِيهِ وَحَقُّ الْمَالِكِ فِي الثَّوْبِ قَائِمٌ صُورَةً لَا مَعْنَى أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مَضْمُونٍ عَلَى السَّارِقِ بِالْهَلَاكِ فَرَجَّحْنَا جَانِبَ السَّارِقِ بِخِلَافِ الْغَصْبِ لِأَنَّ حَقَّ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا قَائِمٌ صُورَةً وَمَعْنَى فَاسْتَوَيَا مِنْ هَذَا الْوَجْهِ فَرَجَّحْنَا جَانِبَ الْمَالِكِ لِمَا ذَكَرْنَا
And if he steals a cloak and dyes it red, he is amputated, and the cloak is not taken from him, and he does not guarantee the value of the cloth. This is according to Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: The cloak is taken from him, and he is given what the dye increased in it, drawing an analogy to the case of 'asab (dye plant). The common link between them is that the cloth remains the original basis and the dye is subsidiary. Their (Abu Hanifah and Abu Yusuf's) argument is that the dye is established in form and meaning, such that if the original owner wanted to take it dyed, he would have to compensate for the increase in value. But the owner's right in the cloth is established in form only, not in meaning. Do you not see that it is not guaranteed against the thief if it perishes? So we gave preference to the thief's side, unlike the case of ghasp (usurpation), because in ghasp the right of each (usurper and owner) is established in form and meaning, so they are equal in this respect, and we gave preference to the owner's side for the reason we mentioned.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage covers a thief who steals a cloth and dyes it red. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the thief's hand is cut, but the dyed cloth is not taken from him, nor is he liable for its value. According to Imam Muhammad, the cloth must be returned to the owner, who must pay for the increase in value due to the dye. Muhammad draws analogy to ghasb (usurpation) where the cloth is returned with compensation. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf distinguish: in theft, the owner's right in the cloth is only formal (the cloth itself) but not in meaning (the value is dominated by the dye), and the thief is not liable if the cloth perishes (since he is not a guarantor). Therefore, the preference is given to the thief. In ghasb, both parties have equal substantive rights, so the owner's right prevails. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
صَبَغَ (sabagha): To dye the cloth, here specifically with red dye.
قُبّ (qubb): A cloak or garment, the object of theft.
غَصْب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking property by force without right, distinguished from theft in this ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what happens to a cloak that a thief steals and dyes red?
A) The cloth is returned to the owner and the thief compensates for the dye.
B) The cloth is not returned and the thief is not liable for its value.
C) The cloth is returned but the owner must pay for the dye.
D) The cloth is destroyed and the thief pays full value.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a thief alters stolen property by dyeing, the property remains with the thief because the owner's right is only formal, a departure from the rule in ghasb.

Transformation of Stolen Property: Coins and Red Dye
Intra-Hanafi dispute on transformation of stolen property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on a thief transforming stolen gold/silver into coins and a stolen garment by dyeing it red, and explain the qiyas with ghasb.

Core Idea
A thief's transformation does not transfer ownership; the thief's right is preferred because he is not liable for destruction.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute from the Hidayah. The key legal question: when a thief alters stolen property, does he become the owner? The answer affects both the hadd punishment and restitution. We examine the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text covers two cases: (1) a thief steals gold/silver and makes it into coins; (2) a thief steals a garment and dyes it red. Both involve transformation of the stolen item.
2
State the ruling of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf for the red dye case
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the thief's hand is cut, the garment is not taken from him, and he does not guarantee its value. The owner's right is only formal.
The same ruling applies to the coins case according to Abu Hanifa.
3
State Muhammad al-Shaybani's objection
Muhammad argues that the garment should be taken from the thief and he should be compensated for the increase in value due to the dye, by analogy with usurpation (ghasb).
For coins, Muhammad holds the thief becomes owner, so no cutting? The text implies disagreement.
4
Analyze the qiyas and the rejoinder
Muhammad's qiyas: asl = usurper dyes garment (returned with compensation); far' = thief dyes garment; illa = garment remains asl, dye is tabi'. But Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf reject this: in ghasb both owner and usurper have substantive rights; in theft, only thief has substantive right (owner not liable for destruction), so thief's side prevails.
This is istihsan overriding qiyas.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the thief does not gain ownership, so the hadd is applied and the property is restored to the owner. Under Muhammad, the thief may keep the property (coins) or receive compensation for the dye, altering restitution.

کو سیاہ رنگ سے رنگ دیا ہو تو دو اماموں یعنی امام ابو حنیفہ اور امام محمد رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک اس سے لے لیا جائے گا۔ لیکن امام ابو یوسف کے نزدیک دونوں رنگ یعنی سرخ ہو یا سیاہ کی صورتیں یکساں ہیں۔ کیونکہ ابو یوسف کے نزدیک سیاہ رنگ سے کپڑے میں اسی طرح کی زیادتی ہو جاتی ہے۔ جس طرح سرخ رنگ کے رنگنے سے ہوتی ہے۔ اور امام محمد کے نزدیک بھی اگر چہ سیاہی بھی سرخی کی طرح ہی زیادتی ہوتی ہے لیکن رنگ چونکہ کپڑے کے تابع ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے کپڑے سے اصل مالک کا حق ختم نہیں ہو سکتا ہے اور امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک سیاہی سے کمی اور نقصان آجاتا ہے۔ اس لئے یہ رنگ مالک کے حق کو ختم نہیں کر سکتا ہے۔ فائدہ محققین نے کہا ہے کہ امام صاحب اور صاحبین کا یہ اختلاف اصلی نہیں ہے بلکہ اپنے اپنے زمانہ کی حالت ہے کیونکہ امام اعظم کے زمانہ میں بنی امیہ کی حکومت میں سرخ رنگ کی قدر اور اہمیت تھی اور سیاہ رنگ ایک قسم کا عیب شمار ہوتا تھا لیکن صاحبین کے زمانہ میں عباسیہ کی سلطنت میں سیاہ رنگ کی قدر ہو گئی۔ اس لئے یہ اختلاف زمانہ کے اعتبار سے ہے۔ تشریح وَإِنْ صَبَغَهُ أَسْوَدَ أُخِذَ مِنْهُ فِي الْمَذْهَبَيْنِ يَعْنِي عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ الخ مطلب ترجمہ سے واضح ہے۔
If a thief steals gold or silver whose value necessitates amputation, then makes them into dirhams or dinars, then his hand is cut and the coins are returned to the owner according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say the owner has no right to take the coins because the thief has become the owner through the transformation. Similarly, if a thief steals a garment and dyes it red, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf his hand is cut, the garment is not taken from him, and he does not guarantee its value. Muhammad says the garment is taken from him and he is given the increase in value due to the dye, by analogy with usurpation (ghasb). The reasoning is that in theft the owner's right is only in form, not in substance, as the thief is not liable for destruction, so the thief's side is preferred; in ghasb both rights are substantive, so the owner's side is preferred. If the garment is dyed black, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad it is taken from the thief, while Abu Yusuf considers it the same as red dye. The scholars note that this difference may be due to the value of colors in different eras.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a thief who transforms stolen property before the hadd punishment is carried out. The first issue: a thief steals gold or silver that would necessitate amputation (qat') and then manufactures it into dirhams or dinars. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the thief does not become the owner of the new coins; therefore, his hand is cut and the coins are returned to the original owner. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the thief becomes the owner because the manufacturing is a valuable act that changes the substance, so the owner has no claim to the coins. The second issue involves a thief who steals a garment and dyes it red. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that the hand is cut, the garment is not taken from the thief (i.e., he keeps it), and he is not liable for its value because the owner's right is only formal (surah) not substantive (ma'na)—the thief is not liable if the garment perishes. Muhammad analogizes this to the case of usurpation (ghasb) where a usurper dyes a garment: in ghasb, the garment is returned to the owner with compensation for the increase. Muhammad's qiyas is rejected by Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf who note that in ghasb both parties' rights are substantive, making them equal, so the owner's right prevails. In theft, only the thief has substantive right, so he prevails. The third issue: if the dye is black, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad agree the garment is taken from the thief (since black dye is considered a defect in Abu Hanifa's time, and an addition in Muhammad's time), while Abu Yusuf treats black the same as red. The commentators note that the ikhtilaf reflects the customary value of colors in different eras (Umayyad vs Abbasid).

Key Terms
قطع (qat'): Amputation of the thief's hand as a hadd punishment for theft.
غصب (ghasb): Unlawful taking of another's property with the intention of ownership, often by force.
صَبْغ (sabgh): Dyeing, specifically the act of coloring cloth.
تابع (tabi'): Something that is subsidiary or follows the principal; here, the dye is subordinate to the cloth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the core reason why Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule that a thief who dyes a garment red does not have to return the garment?
A) The dye is a valuable addition that transfers ownership to the thief.
B) The owner's right in the garment is only formal, not substantive, because the thief is not liable if it is destroyed.
C) The thief has already been punished by amputation, so the garment becomes his property.
D) The garment is considered destroyed by the dyeing process.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a thief's transformation of stolen property does not automatically transfer ownership; the hadd remains and restitution depends on whether the owner's right is substantive or formal.
